DUKE'S BRIDE
IN THE TOMBS
Â«
Arrested in New York on a
Texas Indictment and for
Want of Bail Put in Cell
NEW YORK, Jan. 24.âMrs. Alice
Webb-Duke, wife of Brodie L. Duke,
was arrested at an office in Broad
street in this city to-day on requisition
papers issued by the Texas authorities.
Mrs. Duke had been indicted by the
Grand Jury at Nacogdoches, Texas, on
the charge that she and Charles L.
Taylor, of Chicago, ' made false state
ments in an instrument, on the strength
of which a stockholder in one of the
national banks in that place was in
duced to Indorse their notes for $20,000.
Mrs. Duke was arrested as she was
alighting from a cab to enter an office
in Broad street. When Mrs. Duke was
notified that she was under arrest she
became hysterical and screamed and
wept. She declared: "I'm the wife of
Brodie L. Duke. His family are trying
to hurt me. I have done nothing
wrong." She insisted upon making a
call In the building as she had intended,
but the detective would not permit it
and took her to the District Attorney's
office in a cab. She sat weeping in that
office until her counsel arrived.
When Mrs. Duke was, arraigned in
the Police Court she was held in $3000
bail. This her counsel was unable to
secure and was taken to a
cell in the Tombs Prison. She appeared
to be very much distressed during her
brief stay in the courtroom and several
times seemed to be about to faint.
The proceedings which resulted in
Mrs. Duke's arrest are entirely distinct
from her matrimonial difficulties.

ALARM OF FIRE
STIRS PUPILS
One Thousand Youngsters
Calmly March Through
Smoke at Lincoln School
DRILL PROVES SUCCESS
Not a Child Hurt in Empty
ing the Building, Show
ing Benefit of Drilling
Â»
Oakland Office San Francisco Call.
1016 Broadway, Jan. 24.
One thousand pupils of the Lincoln
School at Tenth and Alice streets, en
dangered by fire that broke out in the
big frame building, marched out of the
structure this morning under perfect
control of their teachers, in response
to the school alarm of fire that re
sounded through the edifice at 11:30
o'clock. The building was emptied In
less than two minutes. Not a child
was hurt and the splendid efficacy of
the "fire drill" in time of actual trou
ble was proven.
Though class rooms and hallways in
the rambling schodlhouse were sud
denly filled with smpke and the crack
ling of flames added to the terror of
the situation, order was maintained
while the children marched in steady
ranks, quickly but quietly, to street
and yard with the discipline of trained
soldiers. firemen arrived
responsive to an alarm sounded by Dr.
Henry M. Fine, who discovered the'
fire from the outside, the building wafl
cleared of every occupant.
The fire started from a defective flue
connecting with stoves in the primary
class rooms of Miss Rosa Kottlnger
and Miss Mary Jewett. There was a
sudden burst of smoke and flame
through the walls that for an instant
caused a commotion among the chil
dren. With precision the teaohers
gave.the command that started their
wards out of the rooms, while hurried
word was sent to Principal J. P. Gar
lick. He sounded the school alarm of
fire that warned every teacher In the
building. In a few seconds the pupils
were in line tramping steadily out ac
cording to the rules laid down in the
"fire drill" regulations. At exits and
on platforms and in hallways the
teachers itood at their posts, directing
the rapid outpouring of children.
The firemen speedily attacked the
fire which burned Its way through the
walls into the roof of the primary de
partment building, which is on the
Eleventh street side of the school lot.
The flames were quenched after walls
and ceilings had been cut away. It
was found that the flue had broken be
tween the two class rooms, the stove
in each room connecting at the top
with the chimney outside.
The primary building is separate
from the main structure, but is con
nected by hallways through which
smoke poured Into the larger part of
the school. At the time of the out
break, Principal Garlick was In the
basement attending to classes there.
He was warned by the cry of Are and
Speedily sent in the school alarm be
fore danger from possible panic had
been aroused. So quickly was the dif
ficulty handled that many pupils in
rooms remote from the scene of the
fire knew of its existence only when
they saw the Fire Department appara
tus- arrive.
Superintendent of Schools J. W. Mc-
Clymonds was summoned to the school
at once. He made a careful Investiga
tion of the cause of the fire and he
handling of the pupils. Commenting
on the episode. Superintendent Mc-
Clymonds said:
This experience demonstrates, first, the ex
cellent discipline under which the thousand
children were marched' out so rapidly and
under such good control to a place of safety.
The feature of the fire drill Is the absence of
any warning whatever as to the time of sound
ing the alarm. The children never know
whether the alarm Is a genuine one or a mere
drill call. For that reason they are never
frightened when the bella sound. Much credit
is reflected upon Principal Garllck and his
corps of teachers for their excellent work this
morning. We are all glad that the Are re
sulted no more seriously than It did.
This experience demonstrates the importance
of fireproof materials in oQr school buildings.
I am glad that the new ones will be built as
far as possible of brick and stone. There
cannot be too many safeguards against Are in
a school building. That of course goes with
out saying.
Principal Garllck also gave much
praise to his teachers for their serv
ices In the emergency.

SANTA CRUZ BANKER LOST
MONEY IN ROULETTE GAME
Hotel Man Paid Losses and Check
Drawn by Loser Is Protested.
Suit on Trial.
A Santa Cruz banker, who played
at a roulette game and lost $1200, and
a prominent hotel-keeper of the same
city, who drew a check for the loss
and took the banker's check in return,
only to have it protested, were the
parties to an interesting case heard
and taken under advisement by Judge
Seawell yesterday.
J. C. C. Leonard, proprietor of the
St. George Hotel at Santa Cruz, sued
H. S. Demfng for $1300. Deming an
swered that Leonard had sublet a
room In his hotel to J. P. Peakes and
the Vein a gambling game was con
ducted. Deming played roulette, first
borrowing $100 from Leonard and
then giving checks for his successive
losses, which aggregated $1200. After
the game Deming was anxious that the
bank should not know of his unfor
tunate dalliance with fortune and hp
had Leonard draw a check'for $1200
for the gamblers, giving his own check
to Leonard for $1300 to cover the loan
and the losses. Demins/s check was
drawn on the Donohoe-Kelly Bank In
San Francisco and was assigned to J.
S. Harlan, one of the gambling firm,
who left for this city on the first train
In the morning following the trans
action of July 10, 1903, to get the
paper cashed, but he was met with a
refusal. Deming said that he had
frequently offered to pay the $100 bor
rowed from the hotel-keeper, but
would not stand for the other loss. He
testified to these facts yesterday and
Leonard was also on the stand. He
admitted that he had let quarters to
the gamblers, but deniea that he was
interested in the profits of their game.
COLUMBUS BANK ELECTS
TWO NEW DIRECTORS
Resignations of Gianninl and Cavag
naro Are Accepted and Suoces
sors Are Promptly Named.
Changes were made in the board of
directors of the Columbus Savings
Bank yesterday. A. P. Gianninl and
Joseph F. Cavagnaro resigned. C.
Sirocco of Sutter Creek. Amador
County, and Dr. C. Barsottl of this
city were elected directors to succeed
them. This second change In the
board In a short time made some talk
in the Italian colony. The explana
tion given by members of the majority
of the board is simple, being that Gi
annini and Cavagnaro are paid em
ployes of the Bank of Italy and that
the Bank of Italy gave them the
choice to remain with but one of the
Italian banks of the city. They chose
to remain with the Bank of Italy. It
was reported yesterday that A. Chi
chizola would resign from the director- â¢
ate of the Columbus Bank, but he did
not. The explanation given by Attor
ney Cavagnaro of his and Giannini's
resignations is that they found them
selves in the minority and concluded
that it would be better to withdraw
from the Columbus Bank.
RAINS CAUSE LAKE IN
WESTERN ADDITION STREETS
Many Ducks Swim In Temporary Pond
at Corner of Fillmore and
Sacramento Streets.
The lake-like condition of the
streets at the corner of Fillmore and
Sacramento streets during the recent
rains caused one of the wags in that
locality' to turn loose in that tempo
rary pond a lot of tame ducks. The
result was that the following Sign was
posted up against the telegraph pole:
$25 reward for arrest and conviction of any
one caught shooting on this preserve without
a permit from Board of Public Works.
A great crowd gathered around the
pond and the ducks, but the admoni
tion published in the sign prevented
any one from taking a shot.

WAY BEFORE
THE FLOODS
Thousands of Acres of Valu-1
able Farming Land Along
the Sacramento River Are
Now Covered With Water
LEVEES AJtE BROKEN
IX SEVERAL PLACES
â
At Colusa Register Shows!
That Stream Has Risen to!
. a Height Above the Mark
: : Reached in March, 1904
*
â¢ Special Dispatch to The Call.
â¢COLUSA, Jan. 24.âFrom all points
.on. the Sacramento River come re- \
â¢ports of broken levees and of great \
' damage by the floods, which have been
increasing in fury since early Mopday '
.morning. At Colusa the river reached
the highest mark ever recorded at 8
o'clock this morning when the gauge
registered twenty-eight feet two and
.one-tenth inches, one inch higher than
point reached last March when ranches
. from Sacramento to Redding were in- j
â¢ undated.
'. It is estimated that there are at least
fifteen breaks on the east side of the .
river above Colusa and in some places
â¢the waters are pouring in torrents over j
the tops of the levees, something that ,
. has never occurred before. Several
thousand acres of land on that side are
under water. There has been no loss
of life or of stock so far. The Govern
ment contractors who have been put- '
ting in a large weir across Butte
SJough, five miles below Colusa, are
having a hard time trying to prevent
the current caused by the weir from
washing out the great levee protecting
district seventy, the breaking of which
will cause the loss of several thousand
acres of the finest grain land in
Western Sutter County. Those in
charge have little hopes of holding out
against the storm.
On the west side things are al
most as bad. There is one break on the
Davis place twelve miles north of Co
lusa that is more than one hundred feet
.wide and through which there Is al
most a river flowing. From this break
' the entire lands of district 108 in South
ern Colusa County and in Yolo County
â will be flooded. This district contains
â¢thirty thousand acres of land that has
been reclaimed and the loss there will
be enormous. There are several other
weak places on the west side to-night
â¢ that are causing much uneasiness, but
as there are a number of experienced
men doing patrol s duty serious results
.are not generally looked for.
The rainfall here to-day has been half
an inch and from the upper Sacramento
River comes the report late to-night
Ihat the rainfall had been one and a
half inches, that the river which began
to recede this afternoon was again ris
ing rapidly and that conditions were
â¢very alarming.
â¢
RAINFALL IS GENEROUS
FROM TEHACHAPI NORTH
â¢ Sacramento River Valley Towns Are
Threatened With Another Period
of High Water.
Yesterday's storm extended from
the-Tehachapi to British Columbia.
â¢ 1 he heaviest rainfall was in the l#ac
â¢. hi men to River Valley and last night
i* was feared that the State might ex
perience another flood season. At Red
â¢ Biuff the river measured twenty feet
and at Colusa 28.2, which is the record
for that point.
The wind was strong off shore but
light In the interior of the State.. In
Sati Francisco the precipitation was

SOMETHING VERY DARK
CAME FROM COVER.
TENDER BROILER WAS VICTIM
Now His Blackness Must Be Very
Good for Next Ten Days.
It was not the intrinsic value of the
chickens stolen so much as the Incon
venience created by the unheralded de
pletions of the coop that angered Land
lord Henry Blanken. Nocturnal visi
tors to his wayside inn on the San
Bruno road were much given to order
ing warm birds and cold bottles, and
It was humiliating to him frequently
to find his hennery vacant after he had
whetted the appetites of his guests by
extolling the plumpness and tenderness
of his pullets. The popularity of his
establishment was not enhanced by giv
ing its patrons the alternative of eat
ing ham and eggs or going supperless.
So it was in genuine wrath that he in-
structed his trusty henchman. "Billy"
Dempsey, to sleep lightly, with fire
arms within easy reach, and to pepper
witli buckshot ihe nether limbs of any
person, regardless of color or social
status, whom he might detect in the
act of pilfering the feathered property.
,"Shoot low and shoot straight. Bill,"
was Mr. Blanken's final command, "and
keep on a-shootin' till somethin' drops."
Mr. Dempsey's acceptance of the
trust was cheerful. Placing a loaded
shooting-iron where he could instantly
lay his hand upon it in the dark, he
inaugurated a policy of wakefulness
that was debilitating, but he bravely
stuck to it until night before last his
patience was rewarded by the capture
of a chicken-lifter with the goods in
his possession. Wonted sounds were
suddenly overshadowed by a clucking
and fluttering that emanated from the
henhouse, and Mr. Dempsey leaped
from his cot, grasped his weapon and
intrepidly hastened to the scene of
commotion. As he ran he stumbled
over a sawhorse or a wheelbarrow or
something, and when he had disen
tangled himself from its legs or shafts
or whatever they were the clucking
and fluttering had ceased and all that
assailed his ears was a gentle rustling
amid a bunch of bushes near by. Level
ing his gun at the foliage. Mr. Dempsey
shouted in stern tone, "Come out a
there an' come quick!" To his intense
gratification there came the response,
"Please, don't shootâl'm a-camin',"
and from the brush there emerged a
man with a broiler suspended by one
of its legs from one of his hands.
"Drop the bird an' throw uo yer
mitts!" yelled Mr. Dempsey, and the
mandate was obeyed with a prompti
tude that made the rest of his task a
sinecure.
The chicken-thief was registered as
Michael Boltes and the police desig
nated him a hobo. Judge Fritz scoffed
at his plea that he happened to catch
the chicken as it was escaping from
Mr. Blanken's yard and was conveying
it back to where it properly belonged
when Mr. Dempsey's gunplay thwarted
his purpose. Now he is serving a ten
days' sentence.

"He can have his old clock and his
old statooettes and his old rugs by
sending for em at any time," said
Mrs. Violet Stevens, accused &f trying
to avoid payment for the articles men
tioned after she had obtained posses
sion of them on the installment plan
from A. Lake of 2b Sixth street.
Surmounting the blonde coiffure of
Mrs. Stevens was a hat of immense
circumference and fairly swathed in
'bird-of-paradise feathers. The fluffy
plumage lav in masses atop of crown
and brim and drooped In straggling
extravagance over the edges, some of
the pendants mingling with the long
fur of the white boa that loosely en
circled her neck and descended to her
feet. Her tight-fitting gown was a
dream of light gray.
"Why don't he send for 'em if he
wants 'em?" she asked, beaming
blandly at Judge Mogan.
"But I don't want 'em after you have
used em 'so long," protested Mr. Lake,
of unmistakably nervous temperament.
"I want the money for 'em, that's what
I want."
"Then, why don't you come and col
lect it?" inquired Mrs. Stevens, with
augmented blandness.
Mr. Lake's choler choked his utter
ance as he tried to inform the court
that Mrs. Stevens' elusiveness had
thwarted all his efforts to collect. He
delivered the goods to her at 511
Golden Gate avenue, and when he
went there to collect his first install
ment she had flown, and not until two
months later did he discover her in a
Market-street apartment house. The
clock, the two "statuettes and the two
floor rugs had been in active service
throughout the interim, and if he were
to take them in lieu of the money due
him he would be* a heavy loser.
Couldn't she be compelled to pay the
remainder of the Installments In a
lump? â¢
The court opined that she could not,
and Mrs. Stevens audibly wondered if
she were expected to run all over town
looking for collectors to pay money
to, when she had other matters to en
gage her time. What were collectors
for, if not to find debtors and collect,
she would like to know. Too bad that
Bhe didnf go a-sloshin' through the
rain and mud to find Mr. Lake and
save him the trouble of going to find
her. He knew where she was to be
found, didn't he?
It looked like apoplexy for Mr. Lake
as he asked what was the use of him
going to collect from her when she de
clined to be collected from.
"Well, then, why don't you take back
your old things?" she sweetly inquired.
All that prevented Mr. Lake tearing
his hair was the Judge's command to
Mrs. Stevens that she produce the
"things" in court to-day, so that the
alleged evidence of wear and tear may
be taken into consideration with the
framing of a decision.

Chief Justice Fuller Admin

bers of the Upper House

I

â¢
WASHINGTON, Jan. 24.âThe Sen
ate to-day took an important step in
the impeachment trial of United States
Judge Charles Swayne of the northern
district of Florida. The organization
for the trial was effected by the swear
ing of the Senators for that purpose;
the managers of the House were re
ceived for the purpose of formally pre
senting the articles of impeachment
and 'a resolution summoning Judge
Swayne to appear was adopted. Piatt
of Connecticut was elected to preside
at the trial sessions. Further proceed
ings were postponed until Friday next,
when Judge Swayne is expacted to ap- ''
pear before the bar of the Senate.
The ceremony was Impressive and
was witnessed by a full Senate and
by well-filled galleries. #
Estimates as to tho length of time
that the trial will occupy vary from
ten days to a month after it is once
begun. It is believed that when Judge
Swayne appears next Friday he will
ask for a few days in which to pre
pare his answer and when he does an
swer a day or two more will be given
to the House'for the presentation of its
replication. A number of witnesses
will be heard and considerable time will
be consumed by attorneys. A part of
each day will be given to the trial.
Judge Swayne is now in Wilmington. 1
Del., and Sergeant at Arms Ramsdeli
of the Senate left for that city to-night
to serve the Senate's summons upon
him.
At the instance of Piatt- of'Connecti- :
cut a roll call of the Senate was or
dered a few minutes before 2 o'clock
in order to insure a full attendance at
the time of the administration of the
oath.
Seventy-two Senators responded to ;
their names and the last name in the
list had scarcely been announced when
< hief Justice Fuller appeared at the
south of the Senate chamber. He was
flanked on the one side by Fairbanks
and on the other by Bacon. He was
ciad in the full robes of his office.
The entire rtenate had risen when the
(.'hief Justice was announced and re
mained standing until he was seated
on the left of President Pro Tern. Frye.
H<; proceeded immediately to admin
ister the oath to the president pro tern. >
and then to Piatt as the presiding oftl
cer for the trial. After they had been
sworn the roll was called and Senators
appeared in groups of ten before the
Senate bar to take the oath, which was
administered by the chief Justice.

"FAUST" DRAWS
VAST AUDIENCE
AT THE TIVOLI
There were probably a thousand more
people than seats at the Tlvoll last
night for "Faust," and three hundred
or so of them elected to stand, on their
own feet or their neighbor's feet. The
rest went down Eddy street wondering
why the architects hadn't made the
Tivoli bigger while, they were about It.
They missed a rather curious
"Faust." Of one thing alone in grand
opera one may of course be certain â
that every performance of it will be
different. With the same cast, same
conductor, conditions apparently ex
actly similar, the same Opera will
count up something singularly different.
I venture a prophecy that Saturday's
"Faust" will show many yariations
from last night's. Some are to be
hoped for; others would be deeply
to be deplored. Almost all of Sisnor
Bazelli's Faust, for example, except a j
couple of falsetto notes in the "Salve |
Dinora," would suffer from change. I
Again, take the "Soldiers' Chorus" and
I don't remember a more wooden ren
dering.
That was the curious part of it. The
big things of the opera, mostly, went
indifferently. The unfeatured passages
were frequently quite remarkable in
their beauty.
The "Jewel Song," with Tetrazzini's
jeweled voice behind it, went without a
glitterâwhich brings one to Marguer
ite and Tetrazzini. It is no derogation
of the Tivon diva to say that the part
is out of her scope. It is out of Sem
brich's. Yet, it is the last thing one
would have expected. â¢
Here, in a performance that those j
who best knew her work declared was I
impossible to her. Melba sang and
acted a wonderful Marguerite. The lit
tle Tetrazzini in everything before has .
acted all round Melba. Also she has
had the honor to be compared with her i
as a singer. So ran the promise. But i
it was not until the fourth act that I
the singer got her usual acting grip j
on the situation* Then came a little
scene that thrilled with its portraiture
of despair. The singing, too. I have
heard singers with one-half of the
voice and not a touch of the quality
rake in laurels in the "Jewel Song."
Last right there was hardly even an
attempt at an encore. Neither was the
"King of Thule" anything but a sketch
of that charming ballad. Yet, in some j
of the smaller love scene passages, Te- i
trazzini was exquisiteness itself. As a
whole, however, it is apparent that the
role is not hers.
Bazelli again developed most pleas- \
antly. His voice did not stretch to the
full need of the role and he has many j
faults of method, but he has such a
pleasing quality of voice, such taste,
tenderness and sympathy, and withal \
such a genuine lyrical sense, that these j
carry him very far, and Signor Bazelli <
makes a charming lover. In the garden '
scene his love-making was managed !
with a delicacy and fervor that I re
member combined in no Faust of my
acquaintance since Michelena's.
Rossi, in a remarkably hand
some orange and black satanic con
fection* was the Mephisto. Quite in
tine with the rest of the performance !
his "Calf of Gold" failed of much of its j
effect. Yet Signor Rossi is a singer of !
parts, though in him there is neither a |
De Reszke nor Plancon. nor anything j
even approaching our old Dado. His
voice has neither the mass, brilliance
nor flexibility necessary for the role,
yet he contributed importantly to the
general effect. His devil, from the act
ing side, is a rather benevolent old
person, who, however, at Valentine's
sword cross, shows a highly effective
picture of fear.
Bettini, the Bauermeister of the com- |
pany, was again most .serviceable as
Martha. With the aforementioned !
singers she did excellent work in the I
garden quartet. Miss Eugenic Barker |
was fairly effective as Siebel. Signor i
Romboli. always earnest and sincere, i
was rather out of his depth as Yalen- !
tine, but did his best. Signor Rom- j
boll's tremolo was in uncomfortable j
evidence last night. One wishes he
wouldn't.
And now. honor to Signor Polacco
and the orchestra, that did the best
work of the evening.

MEAGER SUM FOR STATE.
Comparatively little Provided for
Rivers and Harbors.
WASHINGTON. Jan. 24.âThe river
and harbor bill is said to be about
ready to be reported. In any event
California's somewhat meager share in
it may be stated with reasonable cer
tainty. Her apportionment has been
made up subject to change, but a
change Is not probable.
Oakland, which had asked for $718,000
will receive but $250,000 to begin her
great work of improvement. San Luis
Obispo gets $25,000, San Pedro $250,000.
The Sacramento River gets no more
than a provision for her snags, $25,000.
The San Joaquin gets her usual allow
ance of $20,000. For the harbor at
Honolulu $400,000 is appropriated to
carry on a great improvement there.
The entire apportionment is a disap
pointment to the California delegation,
but It Is the same treatment that is
being accorded to every section. Only
in cases of the most urgent necessity
is a full allowance being made. The
total of the bill is about half that of
â¢the last river and harbor bill.

dead in his room at Redlands this

He took his life in a fit of despondency
caused by brooding over the illness of
his mother, who has long been con
fined in one of the sanitariums in Red
lands. Garrison went to see her every
day, and this forenoon his failure to
appear at the usual hour prompted the
physician to send for him. The messen
ger found the young man stretched
upon the tloor of his room. In his right
hand was clutched a revolver.

WEDNESDAY JANUARY 25, 1905
THE TRANSPORTATION INTERESTS.
THE railroads are massing a great force in Washington to oppose
the policy of the President. Mr. J. J. Hill of the Great Northern
seems to be the field marshal of this well organized army.
. Railroad officials are active among the millions of employes, who arc
â . told that if the President prevail, and succeed in reducing rates, there
must be a corresponding reduction in the wages of the operatives.
This is intended to effect results at the polls. A majority of the
operatives are voters and each voter can influence one or more other
voters, and the campaign will be carried into the next Congressional
election.
The railroad view of the case was put very plainly and very
, : strongly during the late Senatorial contest at Sacramento. The
Union of that city, in an editorial expression, declared the right of
the Southern Pacific to influence the election of a Senator, on the
â¢ ground that it represented an investment of $200,000,000, that was
as much entitled to a voice in that election and in shaping the policy 1
. of legislation as the farmers and manufacturers. But is this argu
.rnent sound? That a railroad has a right to protect itself from in
justice, and defend itself against measures that have mere spoliation ,
for their purpose, no one denies, but that it occupies the same po
sition as rural producers and manufacturers is not true.
The men who from the soil produce the food stock of the coun- j
. â¢ try; and the fibers for its clothing, as well as most of the manufac
turers, have no power to make" the price pf their product. It is
prepared and sent to market, there to be subject to the law of supply
1 . and demand, and as far as its export surplus is concerned to meet
the competition of other countries. The only control its owners
have over it ceases when it leaves their hands and goes to market.
.'* ' -The products of the soil are at the producer's risk. He has to con
tend against wind and weather, frost, flood and drought, and when
these risks are passed has no power to recoup any loss they have
: "caused by adding it to the price paid by the consumer. To reach a
market the producer has to use a railroad or other means of trans- j
portation, and his freight has to be prepaid, or no shipment. Over
. the freight rate he has no control. He has produce to sell and is
â¢powerless to fix its price. The railroad has transportation to sell
â¢:. and fixes its own price, makes its own classification and takes its
â¢ " own time. It does this without regard to whether the producer gets
' any profit or not. It shares none of his losses, assumes no part of
his risks, and is indifferent to his fate, except that it desires him to
produce tonnage which it carries for any rate it chooses to fix.
Now this situation is not one to quarrel about, nor to become
â  ' .socialistic about. It inheres in the nature of things. The power to
.fix a rate for transportation affects every mouthful of food the people
eat, every piece of clothing they wear, every board and shingle, brick
and stone that goes into the houses that shelter them, and every
; pound of coal that warms them or cooks their food. The rate of
â¢ transportation, then, is in the nature of a tax levied upon all of the
necessaries of life, and it is seen at once that the corporations which
. .fix that rate occupy a totally different place, and have a totally dif
ferent relation to the public welfare and to State and national leg
islation, from the producer and manufacturer. It is entirely with
in the power of a railroad company to say where any form of pro
duction shall flourish and where it shall fade. In this State the
railroads can say where flour is to be made and where not. If they
Â° - are getting the benefit of the long haul .into California of any manu
: â¢ factured product, they can prevent the manufacture of that product
â¢ here, no matter what its benefit to the State. To say that a power
of this kind is merely on an equality with the manufacturer and with
'â¢ the tiller of the soil is to do violence to common sense and misrepre
.sent the economic facts. ,
The foregoing statement is not made in a spirit of enmity to
â â¢. â¢ railroads, nor with any desire to incite enmity toward them. Its
truth is known to every railroad manager in the United States. Com
pared with the rest of the world, all railroad rates are cheap in this
country, and our railroad system is an object of pride to our people
â¢ and is the wonder of foreigners. Yet the evidence is indisputable
that in levying a transportation tax upon the producer there are cry
â¢ing inequities. When that tax is rebated to favored shippers the
roads confess that it is higher to the non-favored shippers than it
' ought to be. It is known that on this coast and in this State certain
. producers and certain shippers of lumber and other articles depend
. for their profits upon rebate of this transportation tax. That is not
â¢ a healthy nor reliable condition for business. The shipper that pros
pers under it this year may be ruined by its transfer to another next
year. It is a system that can be used for the advantage of transporta
tion only. The farmer cannot give a rebate on the price of his
products to any favored consumer. He is powerless. Again, railroads
can crush any form of manufacture and obsolete it, ruin its projectors
and' damage the local community, by juggling with rates.
The President proposes only that this vast power shall be sub
ject to Government control, that the levy of the transportation tax
shall be subject to review, and that rates shall be for all, and not be
â¢secretly rebated for the benefit of a few. It will be seen that this is
not a proposition to lower rates or to raise them. It is merely a
Â»' proposition that this command of the welfare and condition of the
people shall be put under control.
It is our judgment that the transportation interests will do
wisely by helping the President, and unwisely by hindering him.
KLAMATH RECLAMATION SCHEME.
WITH some of our worthy solons the word "recession" has
come to be such a bogey that it is shied from with fear and
much frantic voicing of warnings whenever met. The matter
of the Klamath Lake drainage scheme is a case in point. Because
when the joint action of Oregon and California shall have brought
about the irrigation of thousands of acres of heretofore barren bad
lands, and the marshy borders of upper and lower Klamath lakes
shall have been drained as a consequence, the Federal Government
. will assert its title to all lands so drained, there are certain of the
California Legislature who see in this a dangerous "recession" of
, State lands to the National Government.
Here again, as in the case of the Yosemite, it is necessary to
â¢ call attention to the fact that nothing can be receded to the Federal
Government which already belongs to it by inalienable right. The
tide cf the United States to the beds of such bodies of water as
those of the two lakes along tho northern boundary of the State is
tmdifputed and when a portion of these lake beds is exposed by
dra-.ning joint action of the Oregon and California Legislatures but
makes .formal asseveration of the Federal Government's right to the
Though Oregon's interest in the proposed reclamation project
is paramount, California has a great deal to gain from *he successful
prosecution of the work. By the lowering of the levels of upper and
lower Klamath lakes vast reaches of land in Modoc and Siskiyou
counties in this State and of Klamath County in the neighboring
commonwealth will be turned over to irrigation and made a rich
grass country for stock. The proposed engineering works will neces
sitate the diversion of waters from one State to another. The Gov
ernment reclamation service has made provision for conducting the
.work. It needs only the joint action of the two Legislatures in af
firming the Federal title to marsh lands thus drained to insure the im
mediate commencement of operations. A quibble over "recession"
should not be allowed to divert attention from the main ends in
view.
No change in the South is so great or so promising for lasting effect
as the immigration that is gradually and steadily turning thither. The Com
missioner of Immigration has considered plans for reducing urban congestion
by suggesting immigration to the South. Railroad agents are at work in the
same direction. Already the process has become visible. In the growth
of Southern manufacturing interests white labor la conspicuous, while in agri
cultural pursuits the presence of Italians chiefly Is marked by a sufficiency
of numbers to indicate the trend of development,âlndianapolis News.

BREAK O' DAY
HARRISON SMITH
He made his way toward the
sound and beheld Miss Curler.
* +
WHEN Preston stepped out
into the gray November
morning the camp was wrap
ped in silence. Far away to
the east a streak of gold her
alded the coming day. He paused for
a moment to watch a flock of geese
high above his head, winging their
way southward and listening to their
hoarse cries drifting down through
the still morning air.
The giant pines stood grim and
black against the sky and the little
group of log shelters seemed timid
pygmies nestling beneath them for
protection. There was a touch of
frost in the air which set the man's
teeth chattering. He picked up his
gun and strode briskly through the
underbrush toward the little pond
some rods back of the camp.
It was an ideal morning for black
duck, and unless he was vastly mis
taken, he told himself, there should
be quite a flock of them
abou,t the pond. He jammed a shell
into either barrel of the gun and
snapping the breech shut, he began to
move cautiously through the woods,
carefully avoiding the broken twigs
lest the noise of his footsteps should
alarm his quarry.
The last few yards between the
t amp and the pond he covered on his
hands and knees, creeping silently for
ward and pausing now and then to
peer through the half light and listen
intently.
When finally he reached the pond
the band of gold along the eastern
horizon had widened and the light
whs considerably stronger. There were
the ducks, just as he had expected.
In the middle of the pond was a large
flock, while close at hand a dozen
black figures lay quietly on the sur
face of the water.
Very cautiously he raised the gun,
took a long, cool aim at the nearest
birds and pulled the left barrel. To
his unbounded amazement not a bird
rose from the nearest group; the large
flock in the middle of the pond rose
with a great flapping of wings and
hoarse croaks of alarm. He fired at
them with the right barrel, but the
distance was too great for the shot to
inflict any apparent damage on the
retreating flock.
He jumped to his feet and stood
staring at the dozen black dots still
motionless on the water. To his In

1 finite chagrin he discovered that those
twelve motionless figures were canvas
decoys, two of them with gaping sides
where his shot had told. He had
wasted his ammunition on the decoys
| while the ducks themselves had flown
away unharmed.
As the ridiculous truth flashed upon
j him Preston uttered a single word
j explosivelyâa short, expressive word.
|In fact, he said "Damn!" and the
manner in which he said it left no
doubt as to his meaning.
As he turned disgustedly from the
pond a peal of merry laughter echoed
from the underbrush. He made his
way toward the sound and beheld
Miss Cuyler seated on the ground, a
gun across her knees and tears of
merriment streaming down her face.
Preston stared at her in amazed help
lessness.
"You here?" he muttered incredu
lously.
The girl held her sides.
"I made an early start for black
j duck, too," she explained, "and -while
I was waiting for the flock to come in
nearer, you came stalking along and
âandâ" She went into another spasm
of merriment.
J"I cameâl sawâand oh! I heard!"
I she gasped, breathless from laughter. ;
j "You heard?" Preston said in dismay. ;
j"I didn't, really intend to say that. It j
: slipped out before I thought."
"I see," she said mockingly. "You'd
' better get the punt and flsh out those
damaged decoys or the story of your
, adventure may get around."
Preston turned to her questioningly.
"Aren't you going to tell the story
to the camp?" he asked.
"No," she said smilingly.
He looked at her with undisguised
relief.
"You are a trump!" he said emphati- '
cally.
She laughed lightly.
I "So long as I've frightened all the
ducks outVf the vicinity with my can
, nonading, I suppose we may as well go
back to camp for breakfast," he sug
gested.
He helped her to her feet and they
trudged back through the underbrush.
Preston noted that she was doubly
pretty with her short skirt and shoot
ing Jacket and the frosty air bringing
out the color in her cheeks. And, of
course, he told himself bitterly, it must
needs be before her, of all women in
the world, that he had made an idiot
of himself.
Half way back to camp the girl
turned to him impulsively.
"I'm going to make a confession,"
she said.
They paused under a tall pine. The
first rays of the sun were lighting the
tree tops.
"You didn't blow holes in both those
decoys," she said. "I fixed the first one
just as you did, an hour before you
came. It was very dark then, you see."
she added by way of extenuation.
"You took a shot at the decoys, too?" |
he crtod.
She"odded slowly.
"You are very generous to tell .me
that," he said with considerable
warmth.
"It's only fair to tell you." she said,
and then added, "You looked so funny
and It would make such a beautiful
story to tell the camp! Now you have
a story on me equally as good. That
will hold my tongue in check."
Preston looked at her gratefully. !
"I'll tell you what let's do," he said
suddenly. "Let's pool our secrets."
"Our duck secrets?" she asked.
"All our secrets," he said, taking her
hand and drawing her to him. "Let's
establish a community of interestsâ
for two."
The girl's glance dropped.
"Ifâif you think bestâ" she began.
"I do," he declared earnestly. "Now
we'll go back to camp and announce it.
It will make almost as good a story as
the one they will never hear."

She Is the Only
Woman Clerk of
Federal Court.
MISS CARRIE DAVISON.
nl S S CARRIE DAVISON,
daughter of the late Darius J.
Davison of Detroit, who has
just been appointed clerk of
the United States District
Court at Detroit, is the only woman
in the United States honored with
such an office. Her appointment by
Judge Swan comes as a reward for her
meritorious services and follows her
work as deputy clerk of the court.
Miss Davison first entered the office
as a clerk for her father in 1899, and
the following year she was appointed
a deputy.

TO KEEP YOUNG/
Keep in the sunlight; nothing beau
tiful or sweet grows or ripens in the
darkness.
Avoid fear in all its varied forms of
expression. It is the greatest enemy of
the human race.
Avoid excesses of all kinds; they are
Injurious. The long life must be a
temperate, regular life.
Don't live to eat, but eat to live. \
Many of our ills are due to overeating,
to eating the wrong things and to Ir
regular eating.^
Don't allow yourself to think on
your birthday that you are a year
older and so much nearer the end.
Never look on the dark side; take
sunny views of everything; a sunny
thought drives away the shadows.
Be a child; live simply and naturally
and keep clear of entangling alliances
and complications of all kinds.
Cultivate the spirit of contentment;
all discontent and dissatisfaction bring
age furrows prematurely to the face.
Form a habit of throwing off before
going to bed at night all the cares and
anxieties of the dayâeverything which
can possibly cause mental wear and
tear or deprive you of rest. âChicago
News.
Italy's Population Grows.
In Italy there are 172,000 skilled work
men engaged in the manufacture of
silk. In 1902 800,000 of her people emi
grated. More than 3,000,000 of her peo
ple have left their country during the
last ten years, yet there is a gradual
increase in population.

THE SMART SET
In the white and gold ballroom of the
i St. Francis one of the brilliant private
! balls of the winter was given with Mrs.
j George Whittell and Miss Florence
' Whittell as the hostesses.
The guests were received In the
drawing-room adjoining the ballroom,
where great bunches of carnations
stood about In brass vases. Fair, in
all truth, was the young hostess in a
fetching frock of white chiffon satin
and gold, displaying a superb poise,
gained, of course, from the big out-of
doorsâwherein Miss Whittell spends
most of her waking hours.
The guests, numbering about one
hundred, arrived at, the late hour, re
cently established as good form. And
jit was not until nearly midnight that
the ball was in full swing.
Preceding the dance the hostess en
tertained thirty friends at dinner,
mostly of the Burlingame set. As at
the ball, the dinner was a gay affairâ
I and superb In appointments. American
beauty roses and a wildwood of fern
graced the table, with a sweet witchery
of light falling from ruby shades.
Indisputably is the Whittell affair
the smart affair of the weekâalmost of
. the month.
Buttercups and daisies, baby-blue
eyes and whispering grasses, all fresh
from the warm breast of nature,
bobbed and bowed a welcome to the
two fair visitors to California, Miss
Katherlne McCann and Miss May
Deerlng, who gathered yesterday about
the luncheon board as the guests ot
Miss Jehnie Blair.
A wise thought, thisâintroducing to
visitors from frozen-up Gotham these
blossoms that grow wild and unheeded
on the hills about the bay. This
voiceless tribute to the prodigality of
our climate will impress the wanderers
as no words could or would.
Among those who lunched as Miss
Blair's guests were: Miss Katherine
McCann, Miss May Deering of New
York, Miss Constance Crlmmlns, Miss
Anita Harvey, Miss Margaret Hyde-
Smith, Miss Charlotte Wilson, Miss
Grace Spreckels, Miss Lily Spreckels,
Mrs. C. Fred Kohl, Mrs, Henry Foster
Dutton and Mrs. George McAneny.

THOSE WET STREET CAR SEATS.
Editor The Call: In line with the
agitation toward improvement of the
streetcars of this city a suggestion is
Offered to the United Railroads, which,
if carried out in these showery days,
will add greatly to the convenience If
not to the health of the traveling com
munity.
So far as is apparent no provision |
is made by the corporation to remove
the rain which falls on the outside
seats of its cars, that very necessary
duty being left entirely to the absorp
tion and natural heat provided by the
tired citizen, who chooses a temporary
rest at the expense of a possible cold
and pneumonia. The concave forma
tion of the seats is peculiarly fitted for
holding the water and about half of
the seating capacity of the car is ex- â 
posed to the weather.

FIFTY-FIFTH CONVENTION OF THE DIOCESE
The fifty-fifth convention of the Epis
copaJ Diocese of California opened at
: -Grace Church, California street, cor
ner of Stockton, yesterday morning. At
10:30 there was celebration of the holy
communion, the Right Rev. Bishop
William Ford Nichols being the cele
brant and preacher.
There was an exceptionally large at
tendance of clergy and laymen and
women, who listened intently to an Im
passioned and deeply spiritual address
:by the Bishop dealing with the con-1
duct of the convention and the liberal
privileges which allowed any one to
speak on the floor of the house.
â He expressed his deep admiration of
â¢ such an institution as the "convention,"
wiiich, he said, was purely American.
.He believed that nothing more im
pressed the Archbishop of Canterbury
when attending the great Protestant
.. -.'Episcopal Convention in Boston, than
â¢ the idea and openness and free expres
"j sion of the convention. In England,
he believed, there is now a movement
â¢for a freer expression of matters per
taining to the church.
Bishop Nichols asked that there be
â¢ no disabilities, but that every parish
'â¢ â¢ might be fully represented, and he made
it clear that no one must feel that he
cannot have in that house a full and
- lair hearing.
. .He considered it "the power house"
which sent pulsation through the dio
cese, and being a "live church," it
' -looked'"live questions in the face.** He
' courted the consideration of the social
as well as spiritual and material ques
â¢ tionjs and hoped that they would have
all-views presented.
â¢ . Sacred music was beautifully ren
. dered by the splendid choir of Grace
. Church under the direction of William
H. Holt, the organist.
BUSINESS IN EARNEST.
At the conclusion of the morning ser
'â¢ vice the whole of the immense delega
tion, as has been the custom for years,
' took luncheon at the Occidental Hotel
and on the assembly reforming in
Grace Church the business of the con
' vention began in earnest.
Bishop Nichols presided and the al
ready large attendance increased with
â¢' th.c arrival of delegates from other
cities.
With expedition the convention began
â¢tp propose officers. No sooner was the
â¢ name of the Rev. Mardon D. Wilson
submitted for secretary, a post of honor
accorded him for many year* than the
. whole convention voted in 1 favor. A
' similar honor was conferred upon
George H. Hooke and the Rev. Clifton
-. Macon, whose past services were re
. membered Without opposition they
Were selected as assistant secretaries.
The following regular committees
were then appointed:

PROMINENT MEMBERS OF THE PROTESTANT EPISCOPAL DIOCESE OF CALI
FORNIA WHO ARE TAKING AN ACTIVE PART AS DELEGATES IN THE
FIFTY-FIFTH CONVENTION. WHICH OPENED YESTERDAY.
Immediately after such meeting tht
name of the clergyman selected must
be communicated to the Bishop. The
Bishop, if he desires, is to designate a
date for a conference, which date shall
be within twenty days after receiving
notification of the. selection. By the
amendment it will require an affirm
ative vote of two-thirds of the whole
number of vestrymen to elect a rector.
The proposed amendment of section
326 of the Canons was carriecW and |
reads:
The duties of the archdeacon shall be those
of a eene-ral missionary in the diot-ese, under
the dtrection of the Bishop, and adjutant to
the Bishop in the administration of the work
at church extension in the diocese. He shall
be, ex officio, a member of each convocation.
Section 420 was amended so that at
each annual convention there shall be
elected six persons who with the Bishop
of the diocese, ex-officio, shall consti
tute a board of directors of "that cor
poration known as The Protestant
Episcopal Church in the Diocese of
California."
Section 430, (Canon 17). which was
adopted, relates to a Sunday School
commission of the diocese consisting
of nine clergymen and three lay mem
bers to be appointed by the Bishop.
The commission may adopt by-laws
providing for president and other of
ficers.
IMPORTANT DECISION.
By the adoption of an addition to
section 561 all canons enacted, unless
otherwise ordered, take effect after and
on adjournment of the convention in
which they are enacted.
The following important amendment
to the constitution was approved to be
finally acted upon at the next con
vention:
Trust fundsâSection 94. The canonical pro,
visions and retaliations relating to the invested
funds and trust funds of the diocese now ex
isting or which 'may hereafter be created, at
such provisions and regulations may stand at
the time of any gift or bequest to such funds
or at the time of any collection taken .up in
this diocese for any of such funds, and such
further conditions -s may have been specified
In any gift or bequest to any of such funds,
shall constitute a specific contract between
the diocese and the donors or contributors to
such funds; and the good faith of the diocese
Is pledged that such contract shall be faith
fully kept and observed. The canons regu
lating the Invested funds and trust funds of
this diocese may from time to time be altered
or amended, but no such alterations thereof
or amendments thereto shall In any way af
fect gifts, bequests or contributions which
have been made prior to such alteration or
amendment.
A recess was taken until 9:80 this
morning, when the matter of "The
House of Churchwomen" will be taken
up, a proposal which has created the
greatest interest.
SERVICES AT ST. PAUL'S.
Choral Pieces Will Be Rendered by
Quartet and Chorus.
The feast of the conversion of St.
Paul will be celebrated in St Paul's
Church, California street, near Fill
more, at 10 o'clock this morning.
There will be a celebration of holy
communion. At 7:30 p. m. choral
numbers will be sung by St. Paul's
quartet and chorus choir.
At 8 p. m. the tenth aniversary of
St. Paul's Guild will be celebrated,
followed by a reception, at which the
ladies of the guild will serve refresh.,
ments.
Arrangements are being made to
place in the church a beautiful roemoÂ«
rial marble font In memory of Mrs.
William Mackay, a devoted communi
cant and chorister.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, JANUARY 25, 1905.
ENCAMPMENT OF GRAND
ARMY OF THE REPUBLIC
Indications That Colonel A. D. Cutler
Will Be Chosen Department Com
mander.
The annual encampment of the
Grand Army of the Republic Depart
ment of California and Nevada, will
take place in Marysville next April.
There are signs of a contest for the
office of commander, with indications
favorable to the election of Colonel
A. D. Cutler of San Francisco. He was
a candidate two years ago, but the en.
campment at that time conferred the
honor of leadership on Major General
Shatter. Colonel Cutler has many
active supporters, and if the large
posts of this city declare in his favor
he will be elected. Word comes from
the north that the people of Tuba
County are making extensive prepar
ations to welcome the veterans and
promote the success of the encamp
ment.
It is better to kiss a girl right under
her nose than behind her back.

CEREMONY IS MEDICINE
Emma Shalich Revives After
Marriage Ceremony Makes
Her Mrs. Walter Bright
4t
When Walter G. Bright went to the
bedside of pretty Emma Schalich of
2816 Golden Gate avenue and asked
her hand in marriage she was in the
shadow of death. They had long been I
lovers, but a few days ago a malig
nant form of appendicitis struck her
down. The potions of the physician
failÂ«d to alleviate her intense pain
and suffering. He feared the shock
of the surgeon's knife, and to the
sembled family and friends said there \
was no hope.
Bright was apprised of the critical
condition of the girl who had plighted
her troth to him. he sought her
bedside and asked to be left alone i
with her. When he emerged he an
nounced that a wedding ceremony waa
to be performed and then was quickly
on his way to the City Hall. The pa- j
tient was found with her sunken eyes
Ut up and her feeble words were
spoken stronger. Shortly Bright re%
turned with Rev. Mr. Liebe of the
Richmond Lutheran Church. The
young man stood at the side of the
bed and lifted the hand of his bride
a trifle as the marriage vows were
spoken. Mr. and Mrs. Thomas Scha
lich. the narents. and a few friends
in the household were in attendance.
The only hope, if any, lay in an
operation, said the doctor. Mrs.
Bright was an hour later conveyed to
the French Hospital. The operation
was performed yesterday, and last
evening the surgeon gave almost posi- L
tive assurance that his patient would
recover.
' She had rallied to a remarkable j
degree before the operation was
formed,"' was his explanation. "She
was not the weak patient that one j
fears will succumb under the knife. ;
She had revived wonderfully and was j
strong and brave for the operation."
Bright was formerly a car con-,
ductor, and a few months ago was
employed as an extra man in the Reg%
istrar's office.
BAIRD'S WIFE AND SISTER
DIFFER OVER ALLOWANCE
Controversy Not Ended in Matter of
Apolieation of Rutli Baird for
Support From Husband.
The young wife of Miles T. Baird,
who !s now under the guardianship of
his mother, and the sister of the young
incompetent, were on the stand in
Judge Coffey'? court late yesterday af
ternoon to give evidence in the con
troversy over the junior Mrs.
Baird's application for an al
lowance of $200 a month out of her
husband's estate. Mr. Baird and his
mother were in court and were
sented by Attorney Partridge, while
Attorney C. J. Heggerty appeared for
the applicant. An attempt of Mr.
Partridge to examine Baird's wife as
to her own income was objected to,
and the objection was sustained. Th&
court held that a wife in entitled to
support from her husband's estate.
Mrs. Baird said that she had received
$200 a month from her husband and
had never asked any questions as to
where it came from. She did not know
that it had been borrowed, but knew
that he had borrowed money, includ
ing from his mother. A
question as to her knowledge of the
trust of the young man's property cre
ated by him was objected to by Mr.
Heggerty. who insinuated that the
proceedings were to be used in the
case in aonther court now pendiug for
dissolution of that trust.
Mrs. Baldwin, sister of Miles Baird,
and secretary of the Baird estate-<:omv
pany, told the court that his income
from the estate was $306 a month.
She also explained how the mother
and sister had tried to pay his debts,
and said, "Before we get through his
debts around town will amount to
$1.0,000. There is interest due. and
people want the money." said Mrs.
Baldwin. Mr. Heggerty asked the
witness whether she had children, afad
receiving an answer in the affirma
tive, inquired whether she thought
Mrs. Baird Jr. needed a nurse for her
child. "I can only reply by saying
that I took care of my own babies,"
said Mrs. Baldwin.
Further hearing went over until
Saturday afternoon.

WILL SHOW BULL-FIGHTING
STRIPPED OF BRUTALITY
Senor Enrico Robles Ready to Per
form in an Improvised Ring in
Woodward's Pavilion.
Bull-fighting, with â¢ the brutality
eliminated, will be shown in Wood
ward's Pavilion to-night by Senor En
rico Robles, who bears the scars of ten
years' work in the ring. He will enter
the ring with the bull and will dodge,
sidestep and do other fancy stunts.
The main floor of the pavilion has
been securely fenced in. It is covered
with tan bark to give a secure footing.
The spectators will witness the affair
from the security of the balconies and
the gallery. There will be music and
other features for the entertainment
of the spectators.
DATES ARE SELECTED
FOR ANNUAL DOG SHOW
Kennel Club Exhibition Will Be Held
This Year in the Month of
May.
The officials of the San Francisco
Kennel Club are already, planing for
their ninth annual bench show, which
will be held In Mechanics' Pavilion
May 10 to 18, Instead of in April, as in
other years.
The bench show committee is made
up of John E. de Ruyter, Charles K.
Harley and John L. Cunningham. The
committee is already in communica
tion with a noted English judge, who
would pass upon the merits of all the
dogs entered.

OVERLAND GOES
INTO A DITCH
Cars of Westbound Limited
Leave the Kails at Early
Hour at Kodak in Nevada
OGDEN, Utah, Jan. 24.âSouthern
Pacific passenger train No. 1, known
as the Overland Limited, which left
here at 3:55 p. m. yesterday, jumped
the track at Kodak, four miles east of
Lovelocks, Nev., about 5 o'clock this
morning, and eight of the ten cars
composing the train left the rails. Re
ports from the scene of the wreck
state that no one was seriously injured,
although several were badly bruised.
The overland was making good
speed when the accident occurred. The
cause of the derailment is ascribed to
the springing of a switchpoint, which
caused the cars to climb the rails. The
accident occurred on a dirt embank
ment. While the passenger cars
ploughed into the ditch the damage to
them was slight.
Shortly after the accident wrecking
crews were sent out to Kodak from
Sparks, west of the wreck, and from
Carlin to the east. It required about
ten hours to clear the right of way to
enable trains to pass.
TOO MUCH EDUCATION
BREAKS UP A HOME
Wife Tires of Husband Who Devoted
All of His Earnings to Improv
ing Her Mind.
EAST ORANGE, N. J., Jan. 24.â
Testimony taken by order of Chan
cellor Magic before Special Master in
Chancery Thomas M. Moore of Passaic
recently in divorce proceedings insti
tuted on October 14 last by Abram
d'Zon, has revealed an unusual story
of domestic discord.
The couple came from their home
in Russia as immigrants nearly twenty
years ago and the wife, then only a
young girl, became possessed with
such a passion for knowledge that
she gave up all thought of home life
and for more than fifteen years studied
night and day, her husband turning
over to her, to enable her to continue
her education, all of his earnings. The
wife, after she had obtained a college
diploma and traveled through Europe,
told him that he was no fit companion
for one of her intelligence and must
live without her.

AT ORMOND, FLORIDA, SUFFER OWING TO
the distressing accident to croker
JURORS RAID
BELDEN PLACE
Sheriff Curtis Disappears
With His Deputies After
Six Warrants Are Served
Under the direction of E. J. Bowes,
chairman of the police committee of
the Grand Jury, Belden place was
raided by a force of deputy sheriffs
last night. Foreman Andrews was a
spectator, holding aloof from Â«active
operations, for Judge Hebbard has en
joined him and Juror Davis from enter
ing the houses to make arrests. The
purpose of the raid was to test the
writ. Bowes was not named in the
injunction and it therefore devolved
upon him to bid defiance to the court.
The Grand Jury is very indignant at
Judge Hebbard's action, and according
to Foreman Andrews the Judge Is tc
be made the subject of a thorough in
vestigation.
The raid was largely farcical. Pri
vate detectives had sworn out war
rants for the arrest of six of the
women, and it was the purpose of the
grand jurors to accompany the officers
and authorize the arrest of many more.
But Sheriff Curtis, fearful of Judge
Hebbard's stern Injunction, disappeared
with his deputies as soon as the half
dozen warrants had been served. The
grand jurors went on making arrests
without warrants in the belief that the
alley was bristling with deputies eager
for commands.
Juror Speck captured two women and
was leading them off to prison when
Attorneys Mack and Nagle appeared
with loud calls on the prisoners to re
sist and louder threats of suits for dam
ages against the juror. William V.
Bryan, secretary of the Grand Jury,
came along with three more of the
painted habitues. He quailed instanter
when the strong voice of Lawyer Mack
spoke. The mention of a suit for dam
ages gave the juror a nervous start
that loosened his grip on the women
and threw his hands into the air.
Speck had more fight in him and
insisted on taking his prisoners off to
jail. He appealed in vain for help
from Bryan, for the women were strug
gling vigorously. Deputy Sheriff Mc-
Manus came along at this juncture and
Bowes called on him to take the pris
oners to the lockup. McManus, uncer
tain in his fear of Judge Hebbard.
hesitated. Bowes was so full of energy
about it, however, that the deputy
sheriff finally complied. The lawyers
then raised a hue and a cry for the
women to break away. There was
much struggling, and finally McManus
let them go.
District Attorney Byington declared
last night that he would have warrants
sworn out, charging Nagle and Mack
with interfering with an officer.
Greater excitement isi looked for to
day, for it is expected that Judge Heb
bard will take some drastic measures.
At least, the grand jurors are expect
ing trouble, and hope to bring matters
to an issue with him.
Policemen were stationed In front of
620 Jackson street and 1129 Dupont

BERKELEY DESIRES CHANGE
IN REGARD TO COACHES
Would Leave the Matter" Open to In
dividual Action at Each
University.
BERKELEY, Jan. 24.âThe question
whether graduate football coaches
shall be engaged to handle the respec
tive Berkeley and Stanford football
elevens this year was discussed at
much length to-night by the executive
committee of the associated students,
preliminary to the intercollegiate com
mittee meeting Saturday in San Fran
cisco. Though no definite Instructions
were given the Berkeley members of
that committee thÂ« trend of opinion
was toward allowing each university
to do as it pleased In the selection of
coaches. In short, the view predom
inated that the intercollegiate com
mittee should leave the matter of
coaches open to individual university
action.
Ezra Decoto and Leo Bishop, two of
Berkeley's intercollegiate representa
tives, took part in the discussion.
Everett Brown, the third Berkeleyan,
was absent. It is not expected Sat
urday's conference will result in def
inite action, as each university will
desire to receive a report before the
final decision shall be given. President
Wheeler has received an Invitation
from the El Paso, Tex., Chamber of
Commerce to send the university foot
ball team to El Paso in November to
play the Louisiana State University
eleven.
Nick Williams, who graduated in
1902, and who played as pitcher for
the Seattles last season, was appointed
baseball coach. The committee award
ed medals to Hans Lisser, Henry Finch
and Harry Rogers, authors of "The
Mon From Stanford," last season's
football farce.
THIRTEEN TRACKS TO MAKE
A NEW RACING CIRCUIT
Seven Turfmen Form an Organization
in Opposition to the Western
Jockey Club.
HOT SPRINGS, Ark., Jan. 24.â
Seven turfmen who are to form a new
jockey alub In opposition to the West
ern Jockey Club met in executive ses
sion to-night in a room at the Arling
ton Hotel and discussed the various
angles of the racing situation.
"You can say that a new associa
tion has been formed and from now
on we are foes of the Western Jockey
Club," said Ed Corrigan, the "master
of Hawthorne," who acted as spokes
man for tha revisionists. Those who
met to peirect. the new turf body,
which will have thirteen tracks in Its
circuit, were Ed Corrigan, P. J. Car
mody, Matt Winn, J. W. Rhynock.
Rome Respess, Sam Wagner and
George B. Cox.
"Nothing can be given out at this
time of a public nature until this body
has another conference to-morrow
night," said Matt Wynn, who peeped
through the door for a few minutes.
Pat Carmody, who is one of the
leading spirits of the conference, said
the following tracks would form the
new .circuit: Panama, Essex Park,
Lexington,* Nashville, Louisville,
Union, Kansas City, Hawthorne,
Worth, Grosse Point, Highland Park,
Fort Erie and Los Angeles.

FIGHTERS SET
A FIST PACE
Neil and Hyland Take Suf
ficient Work to Condi
tion Pair of Heavy Weights
WILL TAPER OFF SOON
Rest Will Be in Order for
the Tiny Boxers Within
a Few Days of the Match
Frankie Neil and Dick Hylandare
still taking enough exercise to fit a
couple of heavyweight boxers for the
ring, but they will soon commence to
taper off. Hyland went on the road
yesterday morning and In the after
noon he boxed ten hard rounds with
two sparring partners. He wanted to
go six rounds with each of them, but
the pace was too hot for one glove
wielder and he retired after four
rounds.
Whether the joke is on Frankie Neil
or Trainer Frank McDonald was the
principal topic of conversation dis
cussed yesterday at the former's train
ing quarters. It happened like this:
On Monday night the boys attended a
vaudeville show and the feature of the
programme was the great train rob
bery, shown on the Biograph. Frankie
Neil was deeply impressed with the
scene and talked robbers all the way
home. In the small hours of the morn
ing Frank McDonald was roughly
shaken out of a sound sleep and com
manded to throw up his hands. After
rubbing his eyes he realized Neil had
the nightmare and told him to go back
to bed.
Without warning Neil swung a right
and a left and yelled "Bang, bang;
you're dead." The right hit the bed
post and Mac smiled, but the left
caught him in the eye.
In the morning, when Frankie was
awakened, he immediately said: "Gee,
I'm glad I'm aWake. I dreamt I was
a robber and shot a guy." Mac in
formed the boys of Frankie's escapade
and thinks he has a joke on him. The
boys are inclined to think McDonald
is the victim, however, by the look of
his optic.
Neil is working hard for his coming
battle and while he does not hold Hy
land cheaply he thinks he will surely
be declared the winner.
Trainer McDonald has him in fine
shape and says Frankie can go into the
ring at a moment's notice. McDonald
has trained Neil for all his fights but
two, and on those occasions the little
bantam lost the verdict. Frankie thinks
Mac the only trainer living. Although
the rain fell in torrents yesterday
Frankie donned his old clothes and hit
the road for six miles.
In the afternoon he and Young Cor
bett spent o\ r er an hour In the gym
nasium, going through the routine
work at a fast pace. Frankie will take
a rest to-day and go duck shooting
with Young Corbett.
There has been but little betting up
to the present Jime. Nell will ap
parently be made an overwhelming
favorite as this is Hyland's profes*
sional debut and he is supposed to lack
experience. Both boys are hard hit
ters, Neil especially having a wicked
left hand. ,
HAWTHORNE CLUB BOUTS.
Matchmaker Young Arranges a Prom
ising Series of Boxing
Matches.
The Hawthorne Athletic Club offi
cials are making big preparations for
their initial boxing show, which will be
givefl In Woodward's Pavilion Friday
night. This is the club's first venture
in the boxing game. It would not have
gone into the promoting business but
for the need of money to buy gymnasi
um apparatus and to repair the build
ing it occupies. Matchmaker Al Young
has prepared an excellent programme
and it promises to result in one of the
best shows of the year. Seven con
tests are on the card and each will be
of four rounds duration.
The main event will be a four-round
"go" between Lew Powell of the Haw
thorne Athletic Club and Bob Cairns
for the amateur lightweight champion
ship of the coast. The pair are in
training and will Weigh in at the ring
side at 135 pounds. Cairns is power
ful and clever and has been defeating
the best men of his weight in the city.
Powell is exceptionally clever and has
a stiff punch. A "roughhouse" battle
is expected between Frank Mayfleld
and "Roughhouse" McDonald. These
two are hurricane fighters and should
put up a fast exhibition.
Two special events will be fought be
tween such clever amateurs as Eddie
Kehoe and Harry Baker, Harry Ten
ny and Frankie Edwards. Tenny is
the youth who visited Hyland in his
training quarters and electrified the
crowd by making such a good showing
against Richard for four rounds and
taking many damaging -blows without
wincing. The winner of the Kehoe-
Baker contest will be matched to fight
George Finnigan. Young Dempsey and
George Curran will meet in a return
match. They put up a hard fight last
week. Pete Sullivan, who beat Artie
Granfleld so decisively at the Olympic
Club, will go against Jim Keegan. This
will be the curtain raiser. Eddie Smith
of Oakland will referee all the contests
Willie Wolf Is training hard for his
bout with Charley Dunne, and these
two ought to put up a hard battle.
Dunne beat a professional "ringer" last
month and Is improving all the time.
Wolf is a brother of Ted and can go
some himself.
ACTOISTS INTENTIONALLY
RUN DOWN A POLICEMAN
About to Be Overtaken by Officer on
Motorcycle, They Crash
Into Him.
NEW YORK. Jan. 24.â1n attempt
ing to capture an automobile party for
overspeedlng, Bicycle Policeman En
nis, who wears a score of medals for
bravery, was deliberately run down
to-day and seriously injured.
Ennis sighted four men leaving Cen
tral Park at One Hundred and Tenth
street in a machine running at the
rate of forty miles. He rode a motor
cycle and sent it along in the wake of
the automobile. To his yells the four
men seated In the car shrieked with
laughter. Ennis was gaining slowly
on the big car. when suddenly it came
to a dead halt and began to back up.
Into it Ennis crashed, head-on. His
machine was shattered and Ennis was
whirled headlong into the road, uncon
scious and bleeding from a dozen deep
wounds on the head and face. In an
other instant the big machine was
speeding away.

Boxing Promoters Quit the Annex.
The development of the "amateur**
boxing game is shown in a recent ac
tion of the clubs. The expenses are
so great that it is no longer profitable
to promote these affairs in Mechanics'
Pavilion Annex, which has been
abandoned. The clubs affected are
the Lincoln and the Bay City.
Woodward's Pavilion is the only
available place and this is controlled
by Alex Greggalns and Morris Levy,
who have "amateur" clubs of their
own.

questions by Attorney James H. Budd,
admitted that the object of taking
phan* Cheung from his home and de
taining him for over forty hours was
to get from him, If possible, the ad
mission that he was the go-between
In regard to the bribery fund supplied
by Chinese gamblers for police pro
tection. Chan, he said, was too clever
for them, and be believed that Chan
was not telling the truth when he told
them he knew nothing about it. |
Chan was grilled for five or six hours,
and .was then taken to a room in a
hotel, where he was closely guarded.

a. room for so many hours after the
grilling was concluded?" asked Budd.
"Well, â¢we were informed that he
would.give up," replied Andrews.

objected to by Assistant District At

asked Budd, and the same objection

rision was reached to Indict him. He

Saturday.
Pacific Mail Wharf
Piled High With
Freight.
The Pacific Mail Company's liner
Manchuria, Captain Saunders, will
sail Saturday for the Orient. The
rain, which has fallen almost con
tinuously since she arrived, has added
considerably to the difficulty of dis
charging and loading, and it will be
only by the efforts of a large army of
stevedores working night and day
that the big ship will be ready for sea
on schedule time.
The Manchuria brought from the
Orient more than 12,000 tons of cargo.
This will all have to be discharged and
14,000 tons placed on board before the
Manchuria is ready to leave. On ac
count of the rain the work of dis
charging has to be done under cover,
and this hampers the work to a se
rious extent. Inability to move the
freight from the wharf as fast as it
Is discharged and lack of wharf space
in which to stow the unloaded cargo
has caused a serious congestion and
leaves little space for handling the
outward cargo.
When the new wharves are com
pleted problems like this will have no
dread for the men responsible for
loading and discharging ships, but un-.
der present conditions life on the Pa
cific Mail wharf is chockful of ex
citement.
The Manchuria will carry a large
number of passengers for the Far
East.
Overdue Bark Arrives.
The British bark Loch Etive, 186 days from
Melbourne, arrived yesterday at Queenstown.
She was quoted for reinsurance at 20 per cent.
The arrival of the Loch Btive led to the re
duction In the rates on the French bark
Neomi and the Norwegian*ship Norge, from
15 to 8 per cent. The British bark Lord Elgin,
out 136 days from Sydney for Falmouth, was
reported spoken and her name was removed
from the overdue list. She was quoted at 15
per cent. The British bark Stork, out 127
days from Hudsons Bay for London, Is quoted
at 80 per cent and the German bark Robert
Rickmers, out 225 days from Philadelphia for
Hiogo, remains at 85 per cent.

Government Positions Vacant.
Applications for civil service examination
will be received at the office of the United
States Lighthouse Inspector during the next
thirty days for establishing registers of ell
gibles from which appointments of assistant
keepers of lighthouses will be made In the
Twelfth Lighthouse District (California). Ap
plicants must be citizens of the United States,
over eighteen and under fifty years of age.
Applications will also be received for civil
service examination for mate of light-vessels at
a salary of $800 per annum. Applicants must
be citizens of the United States and must be
licensed by the Steamboat Inspection Service
las mates of ocean vessels of 400 tons or
i more. J
i Blank forms of application may be had
by applying to the office at 404 Safe Deposit
i building.
Nearly on the Rocks.
The scow schooner Emille Martin, bound for
Tiburon, narrowly escaped going ashore Mon
day night on Dime Point. Her anchor, which
had dragged until the little vessel was dan-,
gerously near the rocks, suddenly took a hold
ing grip and the Emilie was safe.
ThÂ« life saving crew responded to her sig
nals of distress and one of Crowley's launches
stood by the schooner for four hours waiting
to tow her off. The anchor, however, had
fouled the cable. It could not be cleared and
the Emilie's captain hesitated about cutting
his cable. When the tide turned the Emilie
drifted out of danger.
Water Front Notes.
The steamship Centennial, which ran into
the Kosmbs liner Amasls a few days ago on
Puget Sound* arrived yesterday 116 hours from
Seattle, looking little the worse for her en
counter with the German steamer. The Cen
tennial is the grandmother of all the coasters
and is one of the most ancient vessels now
in commission. She was built forty-six years
ago and In her long career has sailed many
seax for many masters. Captain Pierce, at
one time marine superintendent here of the
army transport service, is her commander.
The Pacific Coast Steamship Company's
Curacao left Mazatlan January 23 for this
pore
The army transport Sheridan, Captain Pea
body, will sail at noon to-day for the Philip
pines. She will carry a large cargo.
The liner Coptic, Captain Finch, left Yoko
hama yesterday,'homeward bound.
The steam schooners Del Norte and Man
dalay are still off Crescent City, unable to
make a landing on account of heavy weather.

the early morning tides are given In the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day in the order of occurrence as to time; the
fourth time column gives the last tide of the
day. except when there are but three tides, as
sometimes occurs. The heights given are In

Coast Survey Charts, except when a minus (â)
sign precedes the height, and then the number
given is subtracted from, the depth given by
the charts. The plane of reference ie the mean
of, the lower low waters.

DUNSMUIR CASE IS STILL
BEFORE COMMISSIONER
J. J. Dwyer Talks of Probate law and
Indulges In Tilts With Sir Charles
Tapper.
The Dunsmuir case is still going on
before Consul Bennett. J. J. Dwyer
was the only witness before the board
yesterday. His testimony was of the
same order as that he has given dur
ing the last ten days.
Sir Charles H. Tupper conducted
cross-examination, and, as heretofore,
asked the witness to make his answers
to the questions as brief as possible,
but in each instance the witness re
plied that he could only answer in the
way that he thoaght would explain
his ideas on the subject.
The case will be resumed at 10
o'clock this morning. Expert Dwyer
will again be the star witness.. From
present indications it will be several
days before his testimony will be con
cluded. m
J. A. S. Lowe will follow ram. and
one or two other witnesses will then
be called.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The storm Is moving slowly eastward. Rain
hae fallen from Point Conception northward,
and has been heavy in the Upper Sacramento
Valley.
The following are the river stages: Red
Bluff. 10.3 feet, rising; Colusa, 27.2. falling:
Marysvllle, 14.8, falling; Sacramento, 20.2.
falling.

FISK DISRATES
BEN M'KINLEY
Assistant Postmaster Given
Position of Money Order
Clerk at a Lower Salary
Postmaster Fisk yesterday after
noon announced that he had trans
ferred Ben F. McKinley from the po
sition of Assistant Postmaster at $3000
per annum to that of money order
clerk at $1200 per annum In place of
W. J. Phillips, who resigned last Mon
day for the purpose of practicing law.
Mr. Fisk explained that since he
had become Postmaster, which was
thirteen months ago, he had intended
to transfer Mr. McKinley at the first
opportunity, as the appointment be
longed to him. â¢
"I wanted to hold him in the de
partment," added Mr. Fisk, "and so
I deferred removing him from the po
sition he held until a vacancy should
occur."
Mr. McKinley, or "Uncle Ben," as
he is affectionately called. Is 74 years
old and has been employed in the San
Francisco postoffice for more than
twenty-five years. At the time of the
election of his nephew, President Mc-
Kinley, "Uncle Ben" held the position
of Superintendent of one of the sub
stations in this city. After President
McKinley's inauguration he was pro
moted to the position of Assistant
Postmaster at a salary of 53000 per
annum.
Hull McClaughry, who was appoint
ed secretary when Postmaster Fisk
took the office, succeeds Mr. McKln
ley, as Assistant Postmaster, and
W. F. Burke, a well known
newspaper man, assumes the place va
cated by Mr. McClaughry.
The other changes are: Miss Alma
E. Bartling, transferred from Station
34 In the ferry building to the money
order division; J. A. Wagner, from
Presidio substation to Station 34; Mrs.
Abbie M. Atkinson, from Station H to
Station 36 in the Mills building; Mrs.
Margaret D. Kirk, from G to C, and
Julius Meyerstein, from C to the regis
try division.
Hit by Piece of Spar.
Thomas-E. Priest, a millhand, em
ployed at Fifth and Bryant streets,
was severely injured yesterday by a
piece of a broken spar that was de
tached from a revolving machine. His
ii juries consisted of'several lacerated
and contused injuries of the head, with
a possible concussion of the brain.
Although requested to remain at the
Emergency Hospital by Dr. Mercy
Kuisich, Priest preferred to depart for
his home, which he was permitted
to do.
