"I got tired of witnessing wills for
him," declared John Kay, former valet
of J. Alexander Yoell, in Judge Kerri
gan's court yesterday.
Kay testified that his master seemed
to take a delight in writing wills, only
to destroy them and* write others. This
is one of the reasons that the valet, j
introduced by the contestants to prove
tiMtt Yoell was not of sound mind when |
he made the will in favor of the chil- â¢
dren of his first wife, thinks the Santa
Clara pioneer was insane.
All sorts of suspicions regarding the j
children of both wives clouded the old 1
man's mind during his last years, ac- j
cording to Kay. The valet relates an j
incident that happened near the Cliff ;
House shortly before the father of the
two families died. "Mr. Yoell was lean
ing on my arm," said Kay. "He said
he was going to die soon. Then he saw ;
a strange gentleman looking at us. He i
flew into a rage and said 'The is |
Bpying on me!' I think that he was !
crazy."
On cross-examination by D. M. Del- ;
mas, Kav said that Yoell had often j
declared that the children of his second
wife were only waiting for him to die,
so that they could get his property by !
proving that he was of unsound mind.
The valet testified that Yoell finally
became suspicious of him and charged
him with being a spy. Kay was dis
charged in August, 1899.
The valet told in detail how Yoell be
came suspicious of his children, one by '
one, and how he even quarreled with
Mrs. Evelyn Levy, who became exec
utrix of his will. Quaint revelations as
to Yoell's penuriousness were made by
the valet.
"Was Yoell a drinking man?" asked
one juror.
"No, sir; he was too stingy," replied j
Kay. The valet told how his master !
had at one time bought 10 cents' worth J
of cherries at a market to save car
fare to go home for lunch.
William Klummenacher, who worked
around Yoell's home at San Jose, re- j
lated incidents Intended to show that â ]
the Santa Clara pioneer was not of
sound mind.
He declared that one day the old man
disturbed the house with shrieks for
"Allie." The witness said that when
he attempted to soothe his employer.
Yoell became furious and continued to
shout for Allie.
The case will be taken up at 11 a. m.
to-day, when depositions from wit
nesses who are living in San Jose will
be read. Nearly all the children of
Yoell were present yesterday.
POLICE PUZZLED OVER
A MISSING CLIPPING
Filed as Evidence in Burglary Case,
but It Cannot Be Found at
Defendant's Trial.
James Mickens, alias John Williams,
a colored man, was convicted by a
jury in Judge Dunne's court yesterday
on a charge of burglary and was or
dered to appear for sentence on Jan
uary 28. He entered the saloon of
Gcmez and Gilmette, 412 Pacific street,
on July 27 and stole several articles,
Including a gold watch.
A feature of the case was the disap
pearance of a clipping from a receipt
book of William Schmalz, a pawn
broker on Market street The clipping !
bore the signature of John Williams j
and at the preliminary hearing- before I
Judge Mogan Theodtjre Kytka, hand- I
writing expert, testified that from an j
examination of the defendant's signa
ture on the prison book the signature j
"John Williams" was also written by 1
him. The receipt was for the gold
watch stolen from the saloon.
At the trial yesterday it was dis
covered that the clipping could no\ be
found. It was filed as evidence in
Judge Mogan's court and no traces of i
it could be found after that. It j
should have been taken by Detectives j
ODea and Mulcahy, the arresting of
ficers, to thf) property clerk, but the
property clerk had no record of hay- j
Ing received it. The result was that
when Kytka was called to testify yes- |
terday his evidence was objected to '
and the objection was sustained. An j
investigation will probably be made to j
ascertain who is responsible for the 1
disappearance of the clipping.
Ragan Warns Dairymen.
Health Officer D. F. Ragan and
Chief Inspecting Physician W. C. Hass
ler yesterday went out on a very early
morning visit to the dairies of this city.
Ragan said that arrests of owners who j
permit their employes to violate the ;
provisions of the pure milk ordinance
would be made and furthermore that ,
if the former do not show a willing- '
ness to comply with the regulations \
he will recommend that their per- |
mits to sell milk be revoked. This
completes the dairy inspection of San
Francisco for the purpose of rating
the same and a rigid inspection will be
made of the dairies in outside counties
that supply milk to San Francisco.

CLAY STREET
ENDS AT PARK
City Engineer Reports That
Thoroughfare Does Not
Cross Lafayette Square
NOT ON OFFICIAL MAP
Â»
Contention of Owners of
One Block Is. Submitted
to Attorney for Opinion
Â»
City Engineer Woodward filed a re
port yeterday with the Board of Public
Works to the effect that Clay street
does not run through Lafayette Square
between Gough and Laguna streets, as
It is not delineated on the official map
as a public thoroughfare between the
streets named. The report says that
the land that would constitute Clay
Btreet were It to be continued westward
is part of the square and therefore is
under the Jurisdiction of the Park Com
missioners.
The report was filed as the result of
the petition of property owners that
the city fix the responsibility for the
proper care of parts of the blocks form
ing the connecting links of streets ow
ing to the fact that rubbish and gar
bage was allowed to accumulate there
on. The Park Commission has been
caring for the two blocks on the
westerly side of Lafayette Square, but
has not cared for the easterly blocks
owing to the litigation involving title to
same.
The Holladay estate and others have
been adjudged to be the proprietors of
the block bounded by Gough, Octavia,
Clay and Washington streets, contain
ing six fifty vara lots.
The City Engineer thinks submission
of the question to the City Attorney for
an opinion would be proper and he so
advises the Board of Public Works.

with. Bids are hereby Invited for tbe sur
rendei of such bonds at prices to be named
by the bidders, to the amount of $26,670 SO,
la the sinking fund. Such bids should be

Broadway, in the city of New York, on or
before the 14th day of February, 1905, at 12
o'clock noon, and should be Indorsed "Bids
lor Surrender of Central Pacific Railway
Company First Refunding Bonds."

ESSEX County Orphans' Court. State of New
Jersey. âIn the matter of estate of Jotoii
Winter. âOrder to show cause.âELIZABETH
WESTERMAN, sister, one of the next of
kin of JOHN WINTER, late of Newark,
New Jersey, having filed her application in
writing with the Surrogate of Essex County,
New Jersey, alleging therein that said JOHN
WINTER, being a resident of the State of
New Jersey, has absented himself from said
State for seven years successively. That
his next of kin and heirs at law are the
said ELIZABETH WESTERMAN, a sister.
LENA GEISHJSIMER, a sister, and SOPHIA
STORCK, a sister. And praying for an
order that cause be shown why letters of
administration of said JOHN WINTER'S
estate should not be granted to said ELIZ
ABETH WESTERMAN. and that letters of
administration of the goods and chattels,
credits and estate of said JOHN WINTER
be granted to said ELIZABETH WESTER
MAN. And It appearing that said LENA
GEISHEIMER and SOPHIA STORCK have
renounced all right and claim of ad
ministration of the goods and chattels and
credits of said JOHN WINTER and re
ouested that administration be granted to
said ELIZABETH W'ESTERMAN.
Now. therefore, It is on this twenty-third
day of December, Nineteen Hundred and
Four, ordered that cause be shown, by any
or all persons Interested herein, before the.
Surrogate of the County of Essex, at his
office in the Courthouse In the city of New
â¢ark New Jersey, on the twenty-third day
of February, Nineteen Hundred and Five,
at 10 o'clock In the forenoon, or as soon
thereafter as the matter can be heard, why
letters of administration of the goods and
chattels, credits and estate of said JOHN
WINTER should not be granted to the said
ELIZABETH WESTERMAN. And It is
further ordered that said order to show
cause shall be published, within twenty days
hereafter, in the Newark Evening News, a
newspaper printed and published at Newark,
in the State of New Jersey, and In the San
Francisco Call, a newspaper printed and pub
lished at San Francisco, in the State of
California, for four weeks successively, at
least once in each week.
G E. RUSSELL, Surrogate.

street. Newark. N. J.

RESUMES DUTY
OF HIS POST
General Francis Moore Takes
Up Command of the De
partment of California
SHERIDAN SAILS TO-DAY
Transport "Will Leave for
Manila Crowded With
Passengers and Freight
General Francis Moore resumed the
duties of his post as commander of the
Department of California yesterday.
He was pleased with his visit to the
Hawaiian Islands. He was not favor
ably impressed with the location of
Camp McKinley, and thinks some of
the other Government reservations
there would better serve for a perma
nent camp. The only troops there now
are the Twenty-eighth Company Coast
Artillery in command of Lieutenant W.
W. K. Hamilton, and the Ninety-second
Company Coast Artillery in command
of Captain Henry B. Farrar. The post
is in command of Lieutenant Colonel
John McClellan. As these are the
only three officers at that post, they
are forced to do double duty, and only
occasionally get an opportunity to go
into Honolulu. For this reason they
are not well pleased with the station.
This trip to Honolulu ends the in
spections of the posts belonging to this
department, with the exception of posts
at Benicia, Monterey and San Diego.
These will be inspected by General
Moore in the near future. General
Moore will retire from the army on
April 5 and will make his home in Cali
fornia. His aid, Lieutenant Creed F.
Cox, will then resume his duties with
the Eleventh Cavalry, now stationed at
Dcs Moines.
Mrs. Francis Moore and her daugh
ter, Miss Jessie Moore, are still in
Honolulu, and will return here on the
transport Logan about February 15.
The transport Sheridan will sail for
Manila at noon to-day. She goes as
an extra, occasioned by the great
amount of freight and a large number
of casuals that were awaiting trans
portation.
Following is the list of passengers:

COOPER APPLIES FOR LETTERS
UPON MORSE'S SMALL ESTATE
Present Husband of President Mc-
Kinley's Niece to Settle Affairs
of Wife's First Spouse.
Harry R. Cooper, second husband of
Ida McKinley, favorite niece of Presi
dent McKinley, yesterday applied to
the Probate Court for letters of ad
ministration upon the estate of George
E. Morse, Mrs. Cooper's first husband,
who died on November 8 last. Cooper
and Morse were blose friends. The
estate is valued at $3000 and no will
has been found. The heirs at law are
Mrs. Cooper and hen young daughter,
Marjorie Morse.
Fear Causes Her Suicide.
Mrs. Minnie E. Denver of 38A
Eighth street committed suicide last
night in her room by taking carbolic
acid. Filled with the fear that some
one was trying to hypnotize her, the
apparently demented woman drank
the contents of an ounce bottle of
carbolic acid. .She was discovered by
her husband when he came home to
supper. The police were notified and
the dying woman was rushed to the
hospital. She perished on the operat
ing table before aid could be given
her. Mrs. Denver was 23 years of
age. It is said that she had attempted
suicide before.

BOY FOOTPADS
RAID GROCERY
Three Men in Pear of Store
on Geary Street, Near Ma
son, Are Quickly Robbed
THIEVES MAKE ESCAPE
Proprietor Follows Them for
Two Blocks, but Loses the
Trail on Arrival of Police
Two young footpads entered the
side door leading to the bar in the
rear of a grocery at Martha place and
Geary street, between Mason and Tay
lor, at 11:15 o'clock last night and
held up the proprietor and two other
men in the room, taking $30 from the
cash drawer and about $40 from the
victims.
The proprietor of the store is John
Stelling, and according to his state
ment the footpads were boyish loking
and apparently novices at the work.'
Stelling and Robert Thurman were
seated at a small table in the rear
room, playing cribbage, while R. Car
son stood watching the game.
Suddenly the side door on Martha
place was thrown violently open and
two masked figures stumbled in. Both
were of slight build and wore hand
kerchief masks and soft hats. One
carried a pair of revolvers and com
manded the party to throw up their
hands.
The occupants of the room needed
no second invitation and three pairs
of hands went up under the persua
sion of the revolvers in the hands of
one men. The other went to
the cash drawer and through the
pockets of the three men. In his haste
he overlooked a wallet in Stelling's
pocket containing $200.
Keeping the men covered, the foot
pads backed out of the door and ran
up Geary street to William, down Wil
liam to O'Farrell. where they entered
a building. Stelling followed them,
yelling lustily for the police, and in
response to his calls Policeman M.
Behan appeared. The trail had been
lost, however, and Detective Wren is
working on ttie case.
SAYS EVIDENCE
SHOCKS NERVES
On Friday there may be a sensation
in Judge Graham's court in the
Vincent case, if the promises of Mrs.
Vincent's attorney. Ferman, are made
good. Mrs. Vincent is suing her hus
band, "William, for separate mainten
ance and the hearing was to have gone
dh yesterday noon, but Vincent was
not in court. His attorney. George Av
erill,. explained that the husband and
father had been so overcome by the
testimony of his wife and daughters,
and by the hints of their attorney, that
he had succumbed to nervous collapse.
Judge Graham was not satisfied, and
ordered an attachment to be issued for
Vincent's person. The man appeared at
2 o'clock, armed with a physician's
certificate as excuse for his absence.
It was apparent that Mr. Ferman
could have taken judgment for his
client on the proof adduced of the de
sertion of the husband, as charged in
the complaint, but the lawyer was n*>t
content to rest. He told the court
that he wanted an opportunity to pro
duce all of the evidence in this "hor
rible, extraordinary case," and then he
begged the court to ask the newspaper
men not to use the names he would di
vulge, "because a human life depends
upon it." He explained that the moth
er of the girl who will be accused of be
ing too friendly with Vincent would be
shocked to death upon hearing the dis
closures. Ferman demanded a further
opportunity for cross-examining Vin
cent and asked the court to issue sub
penaes for certain witness if they de
cline to appear. Among other things,
he intends to prove that Vincent made
arrangements to marry his inamorata
before he asked his wife to permit him
to get a divorce.
Judge Seawell, who has had the case
under submission, yesterday granted
an interlocutory decree of divorce to
Martha E. Braham from Louis Bra
ham, a vaudeville artist. They were
married on September 1. Other di
vorces were granted thus: By Judge
Hebbard to William H. Whelan from
Mina Whelan, who hurled beer bottles
at him; t) Catherine Hart from Mich
ael Hart, desertion; by Judge Troutt
to Warren A. Lester from i'lorence E.
Lester, desertion.
Suits for divorce were filed by Nora
Ren worth from George Renworth, de
sertion; Zada C. Lee from William J.
Lee, neglect.
Mangled by Machinery.
C. M. Carsoul. an employe of the
Union Iron Works, was caught in the
machinery of a traveling crane yester
day and received injuries that may re
sult in his death. Both of the man's
legs were terribly mangled and his en
tire body was covered with ugly, lace
rated wounds. He was taken to the
Pctrero Hospital, where Dr. Buell
found it necessary to amputate the
mangled limbs. Carsoul's home is at
215 Arkansas street.
Tells of Indian Life.
E. S. Curtis gave an interesting lec
ture at the Press Club last night upon
the subject of American Indians. The
lecture was illustrated by a number
of slereopticon views. For the last
sir years Mr. Curtis has made a study
of Indian life and his stories and
anecdotes gave pleasure to the audi
ence of nearly 200, a large percentage
of which was the fair sex.
SCOTS HONOR
BOBBIE BURNS
3lembers of Clans Assemble
at Native Sons' Hall to
Celebrate His Birthday
OLD MUSIC IS REVIVED
Place Rings With Sweetest.
Song's of the Man Who
Wrote Verse That Lives
With a, brave display of tartans the
Sects of this city assembled at Native
Sens' Hall last night to commemorate
the memory of Robert Burns, Scot
land's poet. The gathering was un
der the auspices of the Clan Fraser,
but all the other clans were well rep
resented.
The entertainment was essentially
Scottish, There were songs in the
"bonnie braw dialect" and pibrochs of
"Scotia's Isle." Many of the songs
that made Bobbie Burns famous all
over the world were sung, and sung
well. "Scots, Wa Hae Wi' Wallace
Bled," sung by Robert Lloyd, roused
the enthusiasm of the clansmen to a
high pitch, and "Afton Water," sung
by the Harmony Quartet of female
voices, touched chords. Dancing
wound up the evening's enjoyment.
Following is the programme in full:
Overture, "A Nicht Wi' Burns." Fairgrleve's
orchestra; Introductory address. Chief T. A.
Munro; pibroch selections, I. S. R. Tevendale
and Robert McD. Murray; chorus, "Scotland
Yet," by the clansmen; "There Was a Lad
Was Born in Kyle," Robert Lloyd; "Afton
Water," Harmony quartet accompanist Miss
Edith Adams; "My Love Is Like a Red. Red
Rose," Mrs. A. T. Fletcher; oration, subject,
"Robert Burns," Professor James Main Dixon;
tableaux and choral representation of "Caller
Herrin." by members of Lady Lodge;
humorous Scotch selections, Kenneth McLean;
"John Anderson," Mrs. John Madden; duet,
"O. AVert Thou in the Cauld Blast." Miss M.
Gordon and Mrs. A. T. Fletcher; characteristic
tableaux from Burns' works, descriptive re
marks by Alexander Smith; "Robin Adair,"
Harmony quartet.
The following committees were in
charge:
AmusementâT. A. Munro. A. R. Calder.
Alexander X.ing, R. McD. Murray, James
Smlllle. Hugh Fraser. J. W. King. M. L.
Crowe. Thomas Wilson, William Cormack.
. DancingâA. E. McKay, floor manager; as
sistantsâJames E. Davidson. John T. Smith,
Robert Russell, Thomas W. Forsythe. David
Munro.
ReceptionâWilliam Rae (chairman), David
Mowatt. James Sinclair. William G. Lind,
Alexander Brown, Robert Stevenson James
Stewart.
PIONEERS COMMEMORATE
THE DISCOVERY OF GOLD
Men Who Helped to Build California
Extend Open House to
Many Guests.
The Society of California Pioneers
gave a reception at Pioneer Hall last
night in commemoration of the dis
covery of gold in California. Over 900
persons accepted the hospitality of the
Pioneers and until midnight two floors
of the building were filled with guests
and members. An orchestra on each
floor furnished dance music.
A dainty supper was served at 10
o'clock and around the tables the old
Argonauts told tales of "the days of
old, the days of gold, the days of '49."
Among those who spoke interestingly
of the good old times were: William L..
Duncan, marshal of the society; J. M.
Burnett, president, and J. I. Spear, sec
retary. The following worked hard to
make the affair a success: J. M. Byrne,
R. A. D#ane, W. S. Hobart, H. L.
Mitchell and Harvey B. Root.
Watkins Rearrested.
O. J. Watkins was arrested yester
day by the United States Marshal on a
complaint charging him with having
used the mails for the purpose of ad
vertising a lottery scheme. United
States Commissioner Heacock has or
dered him remanded to Los Angeles
for trial in the United States District
Court at that place. He had been pre
viously arrested on a Federal indict
ment charging him with having used
the mails for the purpose of defraud
ing his correspondents by means of a
get-rich-quick scheme.
Brazilio Charged With Murder.
Coroner Leland held an inquest yes
terday on the body of Joseph Brogaio,
the fisherman, who was shot in the
back and killed on Vandewater street
on January 22. There was nothing
new in the testimony. The jury re
turned a verdict that decedent came
to his death from a gunshot wound
inflicted by Andrea Brazilio and others
unknown to the jury. Brazilio and
the "others unknown" - are charged in
the verdict with murder. ,

New York Stock Market.
NEW YORK Jan. 24.âThe most notahl*
fact about trading In rtocka to-day was the
decided Â»Ãb*ldence of activity. Jiven with
yesterday's excitement the total dealings for
that day fell below last week's average, which
was above a million share*, and to-day that
average would have been fuily cut In half
but for- th* spasm of liquidation at the close,
"ho xuarkrt became almost idle at tunes and
the movement of prices wa* Intensely sluggish
. until near the close. The tone, however,
: ahowed evident improvement over that of yes
â¢ terday. and prices started upward briskly from
the outset. Thl* waa due in large part to
the lea* alarming view taken of the course
of- events In Russia. The idea that an im
; mediate overthrow of the existing Government
was threatened wa* pretty definitely dismissed.
There wa* some disposition to argue that
Russia* internal troubles might have the
eviect of hastening the conclusion of peace
with Japan and the cutting off of the enormous
expense of the war. with the possibility of a
positive benefit resulting to the finances of
the empire. But the foreign situation had lit
tle effect here except in a negative way, when
there was a cessation of the foreign pressure
to .liquidate American securities agalnat
threatened losses in other direction*. From
.'â¢'â * influence there was a normal tendency
'owiard- recover}'. In a few individual stocks
there was a resumption of attempt* to lift
price*. But the motives of the buyers in
these cases were rather vague and not dis
closed to the general public. Their sym
pathetic effect on the general list was also
â  very moderate. The renewed rise in Northern
Securities on the curb revived all the long
standing rumors pointing to the widespread
reiults to be expected from the settlement
out of the Northern Securities case.
The gupnosed imminence of the announcement
of tbe Southern Pacific refunding plan was a
contributory cause to the firmness of the
tir.rrimaji stocks. There was a disposition to
-attribute the movement In Eri*. which waa
â¢qutte conspicuous, to a *uppo*ed connection
with the wide-reaching settlement of the
Northern Securities case, which Is a potent

ments, in the market. But the firmness of
the coaler* had a more substantial explana

oemb-ta- of the Central Railroad of New Jer

CHICAGO BOARD OF TRADE.
Future Grain and Provisions.
CHICAGO, Jan. 24.âFear of developments
in the Russian situation restricted trade in '
wheat to-d_y. The result was a decline of %
<B>%e. In view of easier cables, the Russian
disturbances did not appear to be regarded
abroad as likely to disturb commerce. In
consequence Russian affairs were seemingly
given little weight as a direct influence.
Their effect, however, was felt as a decided i
drag In speculation. The market opened easy
under a pressure of heavier receipts in the i
Northwest. May started %c off at $1 1 ,
and there was a further decline to $1 14 %@
1 14%. Uncertainty as to possibilities In Rus- !
sla was said to be keeping the larger specu
lative Interests out of the market. Business
was restricted mainly to balancing of trades ;
between small operators. Not until receipts
at St. Louis and Kansas City were made
known did a marked diversion in the course <
of theÂ» market occur. Shorts, who regarded
receipts of 82,000 bushels at those points
against 262,000 bushels a year ago as cause
for alarm, brought about the only period of
strength in what strength the market ex
hibited. May advanced to $1 15%@1 16%.
The improvement was not long sustained. In
the absence of any demand from the shorts
whose wants were soon satisfied, May re
acted closing easy at $1 14%<g>i 14%.
Corn during moat of the session was dull
with prices fairly steady at about the open
ing range, 44% to 44%Â©, which was %c to
i%c under the previous close. May sold at
44%C48<\ receding, however, at the close to '
44%#44%Â«.
Oats ruled extremely dull, but held steady
on a slight decline. The opening was a
shade down for May at 80%e, and the market
closed steady at 89% c.
Provisions were firm for the whole list.
Outsiders and grain traders led the buying.
May pork showed a gain at the close of 10Â®
12% c, lard is up 7%0 and ribs gained 2%@50.

Boston Wool Market.
BOSTON, Jan. 04.âOwing to the exceed
ingly smaii tttocks of domestic wool available
actual business in the Boston market is un
usually dull. Territory wools are noticeably
scarce. Reports received Indicate that the
contracting for wool on the sheep's back is
In progress in all section* of the territories
and it is estimated that alreudy 40 or 50 per
cent of the coming clip has been sold. In no
previous year has buying been so general.
Pulled woola are somewhat easier. Foreign
wools are In fair demand and hold firm.

Potatoes. Onions and Vegetables.
The general tone of the Potato market Is
one of firmness, the daily receipts of prime
Burbanks being light and such stock passes
out of receivers' hands almost immediately
upon arrival. River Burbanks are arriving
freely, but most of them are coming In to a
few dealers who control the market lor that
line of goods and prices are well maintained.
Onions and Sweet Potatoes are firm at the
recently advanced prices.
Receipts of southern vegetables were light,
â¢i usual on. Tuesday, and fresh offerings of
most descriptions did better. Offerings of
Wax Beans consisted wholly of carried-over
stock of poor quality, which was hard to sell
at any price. Some Rhubarb came in from
across the bay and sold at 7c per pound.

Poultry and Game.
The Poultry' market was slenderly supplied
and firm under a steady call for all descrip
tions, particularly large young chickens, and
Hens, Fryers and live Turkeys were quoted
higher. The day's receipts of domestic stock
were only 36 coops and there was no Western
received. All Indications point to a firm mar
ket for the balance of the week, as it is ex
pected that the receipts from the West will
be light and the storm will hold back shipments
from domestic points. Game was in demand,
owing to the scarcity of Poultry, and some
kinds of Wild Ducks were higher.

Butter, Oieese and Eggs.
There were very few boxes of Butter In San
Francisco yesterday. For several days quite a
small fleet of boats with supplies has been
trying to reach this port, but all have been
delayed by rough weather, and as the brisk
local and outside shipping demand has cleaned
up the market, as already mentioned, the result
ir no Butter left. Under mese circumstances
quotations are of course nominal. The situa
tion yesterday was, not what the receiver could
get, but what he chose to force the buyer to
pay. If he wanted to squeeze him into paying
an advance of 2 or 3 cents he could easily do
so. Most of the merchants, however, chose to
be moderate In their asking prices and keep
their regular patrons good natured.
Eggs were steady, as the storm cut down
the receipts, and store descriptions were firm,
being wanted for shipment..
There Whs no change in Cheese, but the
market was dull and not oversteady.
Receipt* were 27.900 lbs Butter. 468 eases
Eggs and 2100 lbs Cheese.

Deciduous and Citrus Fruits. â  !
The heavy rainfall restricted trade In fresh !
fruits yesterday, and dealers who were In
clined to purchase freely* of Oranges and other
Citrus fruits on the preceding day were holding
off, waiting for the weather to clear up. Stocks
of everything In the Citrus line were ample
and all kinds stood as previously quoted, for
the reason that there was not sufficient busi
ness to warrant any change. Four or Aye cars
of Oranges will be auctioned to-day. Stocks :
of Apples were Increased Dy 3 carloads, and
that description ruled weak, with large lines
unsold. Prices were no lower, aa nearly all of
the offerings were bought at high prices up north,
which necessitates the asking of correspond
ingly high prices here. Large buyers experi
ence no difficulty in shading the quotations, i
however. Bananas are abundant and weak.
It is estimated that there are nearly 20,000
bunches, rapidly maturing, in the cellars.

Hides, Tallow. Wool and Hops.
A bale of new Spring clip Wool has been
received from Hanford. This is remarkably
early, and is more the result of rivalry to get
in the first bale than the result of normal
shearing, hence it cuts little figure.
Hop dealers continue to quote a quiet mar
ket in their line at unchanged quotations.
The Hide market continues firm and active
at previous prices. Receipts pass quickly out
of receivers' hands.

Local Securities Quieter. With Fewer
Fluctuations.
There was less trading in the sugar stocks
yesterday and prices were more of lea* un
settled. Hawaiian Commercial wa* higher.
; selling up to $90 60 in the afternoon, but none
iof the others showed any advance. Alaska
Packers was still an object of some attention
j and was higher at $90 50 at the opening, but
j fell back to $90 later on. Giant Powder made
! another advance to $65. Ga* and Electric was
lower, declining from $52 to $51. Salea of
bonds' showed a marked decrease, amounting
:to only $45,000. There was a sale of $2000
! North Shore 6 per cent bonds at $100. jL,
The Comstock mining shares were quieter,
with prices showing little change from the pre.
i ceding day. There was a good business in
I the Tonopahs, with MacNamara and Tonopah
Midway leading.

LOVE LETTERS LEAD TO A TRAGEDY
Young Man Kills
Woman and
Himself.
Double Crime Is
Caused Btj
Jealousy.
On discovering that the woman with
whom he had lived was playing him
false and owing to the slow but sure
encroachments of poverty, Edward C.
Tidcombe, member of a prominent
Portland, Ore., family, shot and killed
Mrs. Georgia Nelson, whom he
was supposed to have married
four months ago under the alias
of E. L. Jameson. Tidcombe
then sent a bullet into his brain and
lingered for several hours before death
ended his suffering. The tragedy took
place at 945 Stanyan street at an early
hour yesterday morning, but nothing
was known of its startling details until
nearly noon.
S. Richmond, from whom Tidcombe,
under the name of E. L. Jameson,
rented apartments, was summoned to
the telephone yesterday morning by a
woman who asked for the Jamesons.
Richmond knocked at the door of the
Jamesons' room but received no re
sponse. At 11 o'clock the woman again
telephoned and Richmond made a sec
ond attempt to arouse the roomers.
Becoming suspicious he looked through
the keyhole and saw Tidcombe lying on
the bed, his head resting on a blood
stained pillow.
The landlord notified the police of
the Park Police Station. Corporal
Rainsbury responded and decided that
it was best to force the lock to the
Jamesons' room. This was done and
the apartments opened just as Captain
of Police Gleeson arrived. The In
vestigators found the woman dead in
â¢ bed and the man lying by her side
with a bullet in his brain. Mrs. Nelson,
or Jameson, as she was known in the
house, had been shot Â«Â»ack of
the left ear and was almost
instantly killed. From the posi
tion of her body it would appear
that the shot was fired while she slept.
Tidcombe had a wound under the right
ear, the bullet penetrating the brain.
He was still breathing. The ambulance
was summoned and the wounded man
was taken to the Park Emergency Hos
pital, where he died at 7:30 last night.
CAUSE A MYSTERY.
The mystery in the tragedy lies in the
fact that the desperate deed appeared
wholly unpremeditated. Tidcombe,
upon retiring Monday night, had care
fully placed his trousers under his pil
low for the purpose of pressing them,
where they were found In zhe morning
wet with blood.
The floor was strewn with letters ad
dressed to the woman from many male

admirers. Tidcombe evidently had been
reading these letters.
It would seem that Tidcombe awoke
during the night, and after brooding
over his discovery of the perfidy of the
woman, or, perhaps, his financial trou
bles, decided to end her life and his.
He went Into the rear room and got
two revolvers. Both were of 32-caliber,
one with the barrel sawed off. Select
ing the long-barreled pistol he returned
to the front room and did the deed.
When the police broke in the door they
found the revolver on the man's body,
while on the breast of the dead woman
rested a pet kitten, purring softly.
No line was left by Tidcombe to show
cause for his act, although a memo
randum book showed that the pair
were hard pressed for money. Among
his effects were letters showing that
he had been employed in this city by
J. K. Armsby & Co. and by Moise,
Klinkner & Co. since May, 1904, coming
to the latter firm from Portland, Ore.,
under the name of Jameson. He bore
a good reputation with both firms, and
was discharged from the former after
the holidays were over.
Tidcombe formerly lived with
his mother in Portland at 700
Thurman street and it had
been arranged that she should
come to this city to live. He was a
graduate of the Portland High School.
Tidcombe was about 25 years old and
magnificently developed physically.
Two of his sisters live in Oakland.
MAKES STRANGE STATEMENT.
Yesterday afternoon an unknown
man telephoned to the Coroner's of
fice and said that he was acquainted
with Tidcombe, who left Portland a
year ago under the name of Edwards
owing to financial troubles there; He
alleged that Tidcombe went to Hono
lulu and the Samoan Islands and that
upon his return he went to St. Louis,
where he met Mrs. Nelson.
Mrs. Georgia Nelson was the di
vorced wife of Frank D. Nelson, a
salesman for H. N. Martin & Co., a
tobacco firm of Louisville, Ky. Prior
to her marriage she was Miss Georgia
Mabry of Vandalia, 111. She was of
slight build and pleasing countenance
and appeared to be about 28 years old.
Among the 'satchelful of letters
were communications from her hus
band accusing her of improper con
duct in Los Angeles and begging her
to be faithful to him while he was
away from her. There are several
letters from a prominent business man
of Seattle offering her money and ask
ing her to come to him or let him go
to her. Other letters are from a
woman friend, who is a nurse in this
city. One of these letters congratu
lates Mrs. Nelson on capturing the
Seattle man and expresses the hope
that she "will soak him good."
Mrs. Jean Willering of 1067 Oak
street, Oakland, a sister of the dead,
woman, called at the Coroner's office
yesterday afternoon and while being
shown the pictures and elTects of Mrs.
Nelson attempted to secrete the letters
and pictures In her dress. She was
prevented from doing so by Deputy
Coroner Meehan.
WELL KNOWN IN PORTLAND.
Tidcombe Is of Good Family, but Has
Bad Record.
PORTLAND, Jan. 24.âEdward C.
Tidcombe was a well-known young
man here. He comes of a good family
and had been Hying with his parents,
Mr. and Mrs. Charles Tidcombe, at 700
Thurman street. He was a graduate
of high school and a member of Mult
nomah Athletic Club. He was a foot
' ball player, a runner and weight
J thrower. His best success was as half
( back on the football team of the high
â  school.
There was considerable scandal at
the time of his â¢ disappearance from
here four months ago. He had been
employed as bookkeeper for the Tbke
Point Oyster Company and embezzled
a considerable quantity of money. He
j was a poker player and lost heavily.
I All last summer he is said to have lost
j regularly. It is not known how deep
-1 ly he was indebted to his employers, as
1 they have never made â¢ a very deter
j mined effort to locate him.
1 When he left It was supposed that
jhe had gone to. Mexico. But within a
few weeks a number of Portland peo
-1 pie had met him in San Francisco,
where he had been living under
an assumed name. Whenever seen by
Portland people he was with a wom
an whom he introduced as "Miss"
Nelson, who wÂ»s supposed to have
eloped from Portland with him when
he left. Whether he was married to
the woman or not Is not known,
though his mother heard indirectly
that he had been married.
The news of the tragedy came as a
8 hock to Mrs. Tidcombe. She had ex
pected, to visit her son in about two
weeks.
Charles Tidcombe and wife leave
for San Francisco to-morrow morning.
THE SAN CALL, WEDNESDAY, JANUARY 25, 1905.
FORMER PORTLAND MAN AND
SUPPOSED jWIFE WHO WERE
PRINCIPALS IN A TRAGEDY.
AUDITOR BAEHR WINS BET
FROM TREASURER McDOUGALD
Draws Down Wager by Signing More
Municipal Bonjls in an Hour
Than His Associate.
Auditor Baehr yesterday won a $5
: hat from City Treasurer McDougald
;as the result of a wager made as to
j which of the two city officials could
j sign the greater number of municipal
: bonds within one hour's time. Baehr
! and McDougald, together with Mayor
!St hmitz arid Supervisors' Clerk Fay,
have been busily engaged in the task
for several days past. The first named
two were returning from lunch when
McDougald began to brag about the
number of bonds he had signed the
day before.
"I'll bet .1 signed more than any
other official," said McDougald.
"I'll bet that I can sign more in an
hour than you can," retorted Baehr.
"Done for a $5 hat," replied Mc-
Dougald.
Timekeepers were appointed and the
i two officials began operations. At the
I end of the hour Baehr had signed exact
j ly 500 bonds, while McDougald had af
; fbed his signature to 497. McDougald
paid the bet like a little man, but
claims that he lost because his name
contains more letters than that of
Baehr.

DEMINGS' NOTES
MIX THE BANKS
Some Want Eppinger Grain
Identified, While Others
Demand Pro Rata Division
BOOK CAUSES FLURRY
Memoranda Showing Loca
tion of Wheat and Barley
a Problem for Attorneys
James Demings and his notebook
were the peak about which the storm
of conflicting claims for the remnant
of the Eppingers' grain assets gath
ered yesterday in Judge Murasky's
court. The lean little keeper of the
Crockett warehouse records had made
notes highly gratifying to some of the
banks which make claim to* specific
lots of wheat and barley as security
for their loans to the bankrupts and
disheartening to those who are plead
ing for a distribution of a pro rata
share of the value of the 3600 tons of
grain remaining after the Eppinger
failure as collateral for some $700,
--000 advanced.
Demings was on the stand all morn
ing. He was permitted to refresh his
memory from a notebook containing
entries he had made from copies of
statements rendered to the Eppingers
regarding grain in the warehouse at
the time of the failure. Before he
had finished the dozen or more attor
neys, representing as many interests,
were in an uproar, some demanding
the exclusion of the evidence and
some insisting upon its admission.
When Attorney Freidenrich, for the
International Banking Corporation,
took the witness in hand he an
nounced his purpose of discrediting
both witness and book. He drew out
that Demings, after making his notes,
had placed the book in charge of his
wife and had lost it about the time of
the giving of his deposition in Janu
ary of last year. Later the book was
found, how he did not know, and it
was compared with a statement pre
pared by Attorney Chickerlng, repre
senting the American National Bank,
which demands certain specific lots of
grain. Judge Murasky thought the
witness might refresh his memory
from the book, but was in doubt as to
its admission in evidence in support of
various facts by the entries made.
The American National is anxious
for the book to be admitted. It holds
that six lots of grain are identified as
its own securities, making about 25
per cent of its total claim. Other
banks claiming specific lots are the
Bank of Stockton, Wells, Fargo & Co.
and Blum & Co. Among those liable
to lose if the specific lots are identified
are the Nevada National, the Londqn,
Paris and American, the International
Banking Corporation and the San
Francisco Savings Union. These and
others want a pro rata division of the
grain. They contend that while Dem
ings' book shows certain lots In their
right places In the warehouse the
original grain in the lots was taken
out and shipped or graded and re
placed by other grain, which the
holders of the warehouse receipts
have no right to claim.
Judge Murasky has admitted the
cross-complaint of Trustee Henry
Wadsworth, claiming the grain for
the benefit of all the Eppinger cred
itors. The hearing will be resumed
this morning.

ARGUES THAT ORDINANCE
IS UNCONSTITUTIONAL
Judge Cook Will Render Decision on
Applications for Habeas Corpus
Writs on January 30.
The seventy-nine women arrested at
620 Jackson street and 1129 Dupont
street during the raids of the Grand
Jury and deputy sheriffs filed into
Judge Cook's courtroom yesterday af
ternoon and listened to the arguments
of counsel on the applications for
writs of habeas corpus for their re
lease from custody.
Attorney Ach, for the petitioners,
argued that the ordinance passed on
December 12, under which the women
were arrested, is unconstitutional be
cause it interferes with the individual
rights of the people. He gave defini
tions of the words "visitor" and "in
mate" and argued that a physician,
grocer, plumber or any one who had
legitimate business at a house of ill
fame could be arrested under the or
dinance.
District Attorney Byington and As
sistant District Attorney Harris re
plied, and the Judge said he would
render his decision on January 30.
SLAVE RELATES
HER SAD STORY
Leung Ah Duck Testifies
Strongly Against Lee Toy
and Interpreter Da Silva
BEATEN ON BOARD SHIP
Tells How She Was Coached
in Hongkong and on the
Steamer by Her Tormentor
Leung Ah Duck told a story yester
day-on the witness stand in the United
States District Court implicating Lee
Toy, a wealthy resident of Phila
delphia, and Hlppolyttus da Silva,
formerly interpreter in the Chinese
Bureau, in the business of importing
Chinese women to this country for Im
moral purposes. The accused men were
being tried before a Jury on an indict
ment presented several months ago.
Through Dr. John Endlcott Gardner, a
bureau interpreter, Leung told the Jury
about an eventful episode in her his
tory. She was a slave girl, and was
released by her mistress to a Chinese
man who turned her over to Lee Toy
in Hongkong for the purpose of being
taken to the St. Louis Exposition. She
told how she had been coached by Lee
Toy and Da Silva to say to the Immi
grant officials on her arrival here that
she was going to the exposition to act
as a waitress in the tea garden. Lee
Toy, she said, also told her the real
purpose for which she was being taken
to the United States, and warned her
not to let the immigrant officials know
anything about it. On board ship she
was also coached b.v Da Silva, and
when she ventured to remonstrate Da
Silva beat her. Lee Toy was present,
she said, when Da Silva beat her, and
Lee Toy remarked, "You haven't been
beaten enough; you must be beaten
some more."
The woman added that when she ar
rived here she told the coached story
to the Chinese Bureau officials, but re
fused to swear to it.
During the testimony of the woman
numerous objections on technical
grounds were made by Bert Schlesinger
and Samuel M. Shortridge, counsel for
Lee Toy, and Thomas C. West for Da
Silva, but Judge de Haven brushed
aside all technicalities and directed that
the woman should be permitted to tell
her story in her own way. Then Mr.
Schlesinger took the almond eyed lady
In hand for cross-examination.
"You never Intended to come to this
country for an immoral purpose, did
you?" he asked.
"No," was the reply. "I told Lee Toy
on the steamer that I never would lead
an immoral life."
She said further that Lee Toy had
threatened to hang her should she re
fuse to testify to the coached story.
The witness was still on the stand
when the court took an adjournment
until this morning. The case Is handled
for the prosecution by Ben L. McKin
ley, Assistant United States Attorney,
the evidence having been gathered
by George W. Hazen, United States
Secret Service Agent.
, Three other slave girls in custody of
Miss Davis of the Chinese Mission will
be called as witnesse.

INSINUATIONS
DISPLEASING
Executor Bridgford Takes
Exceptions to Questions
Asked by A. J. Clunie
The plasters on the Clunie-Briogford- :
White wounds were pulled off again j
yesterday, and the soreness re-ex- i
hibited to the critical eye of Judge
Coffey. Andrew J. Clunle, half j
brother of the late Thomas J. Clunle,
and one of the three executors of his
will, in pursuance of the examination
in the matter of his objections to the
account of administration rendered by
E. A. Bridgford and Burrell G. White,
the two other executors, had the former
in range of the fire of his questions for
most of the afternoon, then fired a few
scattering volleys at Whiter
During the examination of Bridgford,
Clunie demanded an exhibition of the
witness' private account at the Western
National Bank, of which Bridford Is a
director. Clunie said his purpose was
to show that Bridgford and Judge
Grant had divided the $8000 attorneys'
fee allowed to Grant by White and
Bridgford, for legal services rendered.
Bridgford declined to produce his
private account, and the court sus
tained him. In the course of the per
sonalities which followed Clunie said:
"You have been holding my money
away from me and using it for your-
Belf, and you have been using the
money of others in the same way."
Judge Bridgford paled, but restrained
himself, and a moment later he said,
"I am tired of your insinuations."
Whereupon Judge Coffey interrupted
with "I can try this case without refer
ence to these personal matters."
Clunie proceeded to examine Bridg
ford upon the matter of Grant's em
